![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-57-0.png)


I I I Det N
the man with,


a telescope



![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-57-1.png)

In (1) the PP _**with a telescope**_ modifies the NP _**the man,**_ and the interpretation is


that the man has the telescope. In (2) the PP _**with a telescope**_ modifies the VP, and the


action of seeing occurred by use of a telescope.


Various syntactic evidence in support of structural differences between two different


phrase structures can be found in other syntactic operations.


**Evidence 1: substitution**


The boy saw him. (Meaning 1)


*The boy saw him with a telescope. (Meaning 1)


The boy saw him with a telescope. (Meaning 2)


*The boy saw him. (Meaning 2)


**58** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-58-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


Evidence 2: passivization


The man with a telescope was seen by the boy. (Meaning 1)


*The man was seen with a telescope by the boy. (Meaning 1)


*The man with a telescope was seen by the boy. (Meaning 2)


The man was seen with a telescope by the boy. (Meaning 2)


Evidence 3: preposing or movement


The man with a telescope the boy saw. (Meaning 1)


*The man the boy saw with a telescope. (Meaning 1)


The man the boy saw with a telescope. (Meaning 2)


*The man with a telescope the boy saw. (Meaning 2)


Evidence 4: sentence fragment


Who did the boy see? (Meaning 1)


The man with a telescope


Who did the boy see with a telescope? (Meaning 2)


The man.


Evidence 5: clefting


It was the man with a telescope that the boy saw. (Meaning 1)


*It was the man that the boy saw with a telescope. (Meaning 1)


It was the man that the boy saw with a telescope. (Meaning 2)


*It was the man with a telescope that the boy saw. (Meaning 2)

**Constituency Tests**


**1. Movement**


(1) Nobody liked [ np the books about New York that she bought].


(2) [ np The books about New York that she bought] nobody liked __ .


(3) * [ np The books about New York] nobody liked _ that she bought


(4) [ ap Neurotic] I would say she is __, not nervous.


(5) [pp in his attic] he keep his plants _, not his pets.


(6) Nicky promised to write an essay, and [ vp write an essay] he will



.



(7) We brought _ into the country _**six boxes**_ _**of excellent French wine.**_


(8) Six women __ appeared _**with yellow hats.**_


If we can move a particular string of words in a sentence from one position to another,


then it behaves as a constituent.


**2. Substitution**


**(1) Phrasal and Clausal Substitution**


(9) My father admires my mother/her.


(10) They say that Wayne is _**very unhappy**_ and _**sp**_ he is.


(11) Our neighbours will go on holiday _**on Sunday**_ and we will leave _**then**_ too.


(12) Tim sat _**on the couch**_ and stayed _**there.**_


(13) He said _**that the operation will be successful.**_ I certainly hope _**so.**_


A particular string of words is a constituent if it can be substituted by a suitable


pro-form.

**3. Coordination**


(20) Frank _**washed and ironed**_ his shirts yesterday, (coordinated main verbs)


(21) Frank _**washed his shirts**_ and _**polished his shoes**_ yesterday, (coordinated lower


V-bars)


(22) Frank washed his shirts _**yesterday**_ and _**last week,**_ (coordinated adjunct NPs)


(23) Frank _**washed his shirts yesterday**_ and _**polished his shoes last week (coordinated**_


higher V-bars)


(24) Julius loudly _**announced**_ and _**decried**_ the election victory, (coordinated main verbs)


(25) Julius loudly _**announced the election victory**_ and _**gave an interview to the press.**_


(coordinated lower V-bars)


(26) Julius _**loudly**_ and _**disgustedly**_ announced the election victory, (coordinated AdvPs)


(27) Julius _**loudly announced the election victory**_ and _**disgustedly gave an interview**_


_**to the press,**_ (coordinated higher V-bars)


Only the same kind of constituents can be coordinated.


_U._ **Cleft and Pseudo-cleft Sentences**


**(1) Cleft Sentences**


(28) It was _**Frank**_ who washed his shirts yesterday.


(29) It was _**his shirts**_ that Frank washed yesterday.


(30) It was _**yesterday**_ that Frank washed his shirts.


**(2) Pseudo-cleft Sentences**


(31) What Frank washed yesterday was _**his shirts.**_


(32) What Frank did yesterday _**was wash his shirts.**_


(33) What Frank did was _**wash his shirts yesterday.**_


Only constituents can occur in the focus position.

**id Adjuncts**

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-66-0.png)


**1. Complements and Adjuncts in VPs**


**(D Obligatoriness**


Complements are strictly-required phrases whereas adjuncts are not. The examples in


(l)-(3) show that the verb _**placed**_ requires an NP and a PP as its complements, _**kept**_ an


NP and a PP or an AP, and _**stayed**_ a PP.


Chapter 3 English Syntax                                            - 67


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-67-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


(1) a. John placed Kim behind the garage.


b. John kept him behind the garage.


c. *John stayed Kim behind the garage.


(2) a. *John placed him busy.


b. John kept him busy.


c. *John stayed him busy.


(3) a. *John placed behind the counter.


b. *John kept behind the counter.


c. John stayed behind the counter.


In contrast, adjuncts are optional. Their presence is not required by grammar:


(4) a. John deposited some money in the bank.


b. John deposited some money in the bank on Friday.


In (4b), the PP _**on Friday**_ is optional here, serving as a adjunct.


**(2) Iterability**


The possibility of iterating identical types of phrase can also distinguish between


complements and adjuncts. In general two or more instances of the same adjunct type


can occur with the same head, but this is impossible for complements.


(5) a. *The UN blamed global warming [on humans] [on natural causes].


b. Kim and Sandy met [in Seoul] [in the lobby of the Lotte Hotel] in March.


In (5a) _**on humans**_ is a complement and thus the same type of PP _**on natural causes**_


cannot co-occur. Yet _**in Seoul**_ is an adjunct and we can repeatedly have the same type


of PP.


**(3) Do-so test**


Another reliable test often used to distinguish complements from adjuncts is the _**do**_


_**so**_ test. As shown in (6), we can use _**do so**_ to avoid repetition of an identical VP


expression:


6 8 • What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-68-full.png)

www.amoriteaching.co.k r^ ^


(6) a. John deposited some money in the checking account and Mary did so (too).


b. John deposited some money in the checking account on Friday and Mary did


so (too).


What we can observe in (6b) is that the VP _**did so**_ can replace either the minimal


phrase _**deposited some money in the checking account**_ or the maximal phrase including


the adjunct _**on Friday.**_ Notice that this VP can replace only the minimal phrase, leaving


out the adjunct.


(7) John deposited some money in the checking account on Friday and Mary did


so on Monday.


From these observations, we can draw the conclusion that if something can be replaced


by _**do so,**_ then it is either a minimal or a maximal phrase. This in turn means that this


‘replacement’ VP cannot be understood to leave out any complement(s). This can be


verified with more data:


(8) a. *John [deposited some money in the checking account] and Mary did so


in the saving account.


b. *John [gave a present to the student] and Mary did so to the teacher.


Here the PPs _**in the checking account**_ and _**to the student**_ are both complements, and


thus they should be included in the _**do so**_ phrase. This gives us the following informal


generalization:


(9) _**Do-so**_ Replacement Condition:


The phrase _**do so**_ can replace a verb phrase which includes at least all the


complements of the verb.


This condition explains why we cannot have another locative complement phrase _**in**_


_**the savings account**_ or _**to the teacher**_ in (8). The unacceptability of the examples in (10)


also supports this generalization about English grammar:


Chapter 3 English Syntax • **69**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-69-full.png)

ilM k l


What English Language Teachers Should Know


(10) a. *John locked Fido in the garage and Mary did so in the room,


b. *John ate a carrot and Mary did so a radish.


**(4) Ordering difference**


Another difference that follows from the structural distinction between complements


and adjuncts is an ordering difference. As a complement needs to combine with a lexical


head first, adjuncts follow complements:


(11) a. John met [a student] [in the park],


b. *John met [in the park] [a student].


Adverb Phrases express information about time, manner, reason, possibility, speaker


intention, and so on. The occur in a wide range of positions and can be inserted into


almost any sentence, to add more information and detail. Adverb phrases are thus typically


adjuncts rather than complements; verbs and other heads do not typically _**select**_ adverbs.


Rather, adverb phrases can occur fairly indiscriminately in any sentence.


There are a few cases in which adverb phrases appear to be selected by verbs. One


is given in (12).


(12) The official worded the bill _**carefully.**_


Typically, we don’t just _**word**_ something, _**we word something in a particular way.**_


Consequently, (12) sounds oddly incomplete if we omit the adverb phrase _**carefully.**_


**2. Complements and Adjuncts in NPs**


**(D Ordering difference**


In the tree structure, one can find out the difference between the complement and


the adjunct by looking at which element is sister to which one. For instance, in (1) _**of**_


_**physics**_ is sister to N and is therefore the complement, whereas _**from England**_ is not a


sister to N and is therefore the adjunct. There can only be one complement but many


adjuncts and the order between complement and adjunct cannot be reversed as (2) shows.


This is the first argument you can use to distinguish between complements and adjuncts:

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-70-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr "](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-70-0.png)


(2) *A teacher from England of physics.


**(2) One-replacement test**


Apart from word order, there is a second way to distinguish complements from


adjuncts and it involves determining what pronoun one can use to pronominalize certain


parts of the NP. In (1), _**teacher of physics**_ and _**teacher of physics from England**_ are N's.


There is an argument that the status of N' is different from that of N and NP. It can


be shown that the N1 can be replaced by _**one**_, but that the N cannot be replaced by _**one.**_


In (3), _**one**_ replaces _**teacher of physics,**_ i.e. an N1, and the sentence is grammatical; in


(4), _**one**_ replaces _**teacher**_, i.e. an N, and this results in an ungrammatical sentence:


(3) I know the teacher of physics from England and the one from France.


(4) *1 know the teacher of physics from England and the one of chemistry.


Hence, the first piece of evidence for distinguishing complement and adjunct is that


the complement is closer to the head, as in (1). Secondly, there is also evidence for the


special status of the intermediate category N' in that it can be replaced by _**one.**_


**(3) Coordination test**


A third test for distinguishing complements from adjuncts is coordination. It is possible


to coordinate two complements, as in (5), or two adjuncts, as in (6), but not a complement


and an adjunct, as in (7):

(6) The teachers from Turkey and from Spain.


(7) *The teachers from Turkey and of physics.

**1. V** **+ [NP +** **to-infinitive]**


**(D Meaning**


(1) Ed believes [the story to be false]

What Ed believes is the proposition that the story is false


The predicate _**believe**_ takes only one internal argument.


**(2) Dummy Elements and Idiom Chunks**


(4) Ed believes it always to be raining in London.


(5) Ed believes there to be a traitor in the company.


Expletives that occur only in the subject position occupy the postverbal position.


(6) Ed believes the coast to be clear.


(7) Ed believes the fat to be in the fire.


Subject expressions occur in the postverbal position.


**(3) Postverbal Passivisation**


(8) Ed believes [the jury to have given the wrong verdict].


(9) Ed believes [the wrong verdict to have been given by the jury].

If wc can passivize the postverbal portion in any verb+NP+to-infinitive construction


without a resulting change in meaning, then the postverbal NP is not a Direct Object,


but the Subject of the infinitive clause.

Subject expressions don’t occur in the postverbal position.

(15) Ed persuaded Brian to interview Melanie.


(16) Ed persuaded Melanie to be interviewed by Brian.


Postverbal passivization cause a change in meaning.

**3. V + [NP + to-infinitive]**


**(1) Meaning**


(17) Kate wants Ralph to get out of her life.


What Kate wants is the proposition that Ralph gets out of her life.


The predicate _**want**_ takes only one internal argument.


**(2) Dummy Elements and Idiom Chunks**


(18) Kate wanted it to rain on Ralph’s birthday.


(19) Ralph wanted there to be a ceasefire between him and Kate.


Expletives occupy the postverbal position.


(20) Kate wants the coast to be clear, in order for her to escape form Ralph.


(21) Kate doesn’t want the fat to be in the fire, because of some stupid action of


Ralph’s.


Subject expressions occur in the postverbal position.


**(3) Postverbal Passivisation**


(22) Kate wanted Janet to poison Ralph.


(23) Kate wanted Ralph to be poisoned by Janet.


Postverbal passivization does not cause a change in meaning.

_**1U**_   - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-74-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


All three sentences conform to the pattern verb + NP + to-infinitive, and yet they


are syntactically different: in the _**believe**_ + NP + to-infinitive construction the postverbal


NP functions as the Subject of a subordinate clause, while the NP in the _**persuade**_ +


NP + to-infinitive construction functions as a Direct Object. In the _**want**_ + NP +


to-infinitive construction the postverbal NP is also the Subject of a subordinate clause,


but, unlike the postverbal NP in the _**believe**_ + NP + to-infinitive construction, it cannot


be fronted when the matrix clause is passivised.


With regard to verb + NP + to-infinitive constructions, we can now set up three classes


of verbs:


_**Believe**_ class


Verb + Direct Object clause in the form of an NP + to-infinitive. The NP can be


fronted under passivisation.


examples: [s Ed [vi> believes [clause the story to be false]]]


The story is believed _ to be false by Ed.


Other verbs: _**expect, consider, suppose, intend, know, understand**_


_**Persuade**_ class


Verb + Direct Object NP + second complement in the form of a to-infinitive clause


with an implied subject that is coreferential with the matrix clause DO. The NP can be


fronted under passivisation.


examples: [s Ed [v p persuaded [ n p Brian]) [clause PROj to interview Melanie]]]


Brian was persuaded  to interview Melanie by Ed.


Other verbs: _**convince, beg, force, advise, notify**_


_**Want**_ class


Verb + Direct Object clause in the form of an NP + to-infinitive. The NP cannot be


fronted under passivisation.

**Phrasal Verb versus Prepositional Verb**


(1) Drunks would get off the bus. (prepositional verb)


(2) Drunks would put off the customers, (phrasal verb)


**1. Coordination**


(3) Drunks would get off the bus and on the train.


(4) * Drunks would put off the customers and off the waitresses.


PP can be coordinated in (3), but not in (4).


**2. Range of Selectional Restrictions**


(5) Drunks would get off the bus/the train/the table/!the sea/!the wind.


(6) Drunks would get on the bus/the train/the table/!the sea/!the wind.


Related Prepositional verbs select the same range of complements.


(7) Drunks would put off the customers/the waiters/!dirty clothes.


(8) Drunks would put on dirty clothes/!the customers/!the waiters.


Phrasal verbs like _**put off**_ and _**put on**_ don’t select the same range of complements.

CHECKPOINT


**Control and Raising**


We will discuss two classes of predicates in English. Members of the first class,


including verbs and adjective like _**persuade, order, force, forbid, urge, try, plan, pretend,**_


_**intend, refuse, agree, hope, eager, reluctant,**_ are often referred to as CONTROL


PREDICATES. Members of the second class, verbs and adjectives like _**believe, consider,**_


_**expect, imagine, seem, appear, likely, certain, happen, tend, begin,**_ etc., are often


referred to as RAISING PREDICATES.


Predicates in both classes take an infinitival open complement, whose subject is


controlled by an element of the matrix clause. However, there are several systematic


differences between the two classes which require an explanation. We will begin by listing


some of these differences, then attempt to develop an analysis which will account for


the data. We will suggest that the two classes of predicates are essentially identical in


terms of assignment of grammatical relations and phrase structure configuration. The


crucial difference between the two concerns the assignment of semantic roles.


**1. Dummy subjects**


Certain verbs in English allow the pro-forms _**it**_ or _**there**_ to appear as “dummy”


subjects. This means that the pro-form appears in the subject position and bears the subject


relation, but has no semantic content; it does not take an antecedent (in the usual sense)


and is not assigned any semantic role by the verb. Predicates of the Raising type (1) allow


dummy subjects to appear in their subject position, whereas predicates of the Control type


(2) do not: 1


(1) a. It seems to be raining.


b. There are likely to be a lot of accidents if we get a snow-storm.


c. It tends to get dark in the valley by mid-afternoon.


d. There began to emerge a pattern in the criminal’s behavior.


Chapter 3 English Syntax • **79**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-79-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


(2) a. *It tries to be raining.


b. *There are eager to be a lot of accidents if we get a snow-storm.


c. *It intends to get dark in the valley by mid-afternoon.


d. *There refused to emerge a pattern in the criminal’s behavior.


**2. Selectional restrictions**


Predicates of the Control type may impose selectional restrictions on their subjects.


This is illustrated by the unacceptability of (3b) and (4b). However, predicates of the


Raising type do not impose selectional restrictions on their subjects, as illustrated in (3a)


and (4a):


(3) a. That brick appears to be cracked,


b. #That brick tried to be cracked.


(4) a. Your radio is likely to be confiscated by the customs officers,


b. #Your radio is eager to be confiscated by the customs officers.


**3. Passivization**


When the complement of a predicate of the Control type is passivized, the meaning


of the sentence as a whole is altered. This is illustrated in (6) and (8). But with predicates


of the Raising type, passivization does not affect the propositional meaning in any


significant way. That is, either form could be used to describe the same situation, and


if one sentence is true the other must be true as well. This is illustrated in (5) and (7):


(5) a. Dr. Benson is likely to examine John.


b. John is likely to be examined by Dr. Benson.


(no difference in meaning)


(6) a. Dr. Benson is reluctant to examine John.


b. John is reluctant to be examined by Dr. Benson.


(clear difference in meaning)


**80** ® What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-80-full.png)

www.amoriteaching.co.kr^wc'


(7) a. All the girls seem to like John.


b. John seems to be liked by all the girls,


(no difference in meaning)


(8) a. All the girls try to like John.


b. John tries to be liked by all the girls,


(clear difference in meaning)


_U._ **Idiom chunks**


Idiomatic phrases consist of several words, but the meaning of the phrase is not


predictable from the meanings of the individual words. The entire phrase functions


semantically as a single lexical unit. Predicates of the Raising type may allow one part


(or “chunk ”) of an idiomatic expression to appear in the subject position, without losing


the idiomatic meaning of the expression as a whole. This is illustrated in the (a) sentences


in (9-12). With predicates of the Control type, however, this is never possible, as


illustrated in the corresponding (b) sentences.


(9) a. Tabs continue to be kept on the leading suspects,


b. #Tabs intend to be kept on the leading suspects.


_**{keep tubs on**_ = ‘to watch or monitor someone’s movements’)


(10) a. John’s goose seems to be cooked,


b. #John’s goose tries to be cooked.


_**(his goose is cooked**_ = ‘lie is in serious trouble )


(11) a. The cat appears to be out of the bag.


b. #The cat refuses to be out of the bag.


_**{to let the cat out of the bag**_ = ‘to reveal a secret )

PL


**Phenomena of Reflexives and Pronouns**


**1. Antecedents of Reflexive Pronouns**


**(1) Agreement**


Pronominal forms always reflect some of the inherent semantic features of their


antecedent. In English, all pronouns must agree with their antecedents in person, number,


and gender. Thus the masculine subject in (la) requires a masculine reflexive, the feminine


subject in (lb) requires a feminine reflexive, and the third person plural subject in (lc)


requires a third person plural reflexive.


(1) a. My brother admires himself/*herself.


b. My sister admires *himself/herself.


c. My parents admire *himself7*herself/themselves.


**(2) Domain**


The examples in (1) not only show that reflexives must agree with their antecedents,


they illustrate something else as well: reflexive pronouns must find an eligible antecedent


within their immediate sentence. The form _**herself**_ would be ungrammatical in (la)


because that sentence does not contain a possible antecedent (i.e., a feminine singular


NP). Even if a potential antecedent is available in the immediately preceding sentence,


as in the example below, the form _**herself**_ would still be ungrammatical:


(la') We saw Mrs. Thatcher on television last night. *My brother admires herself


greatly.


Such examples show that a rellexive pronoun may not be separated from its antecedent


by a sentence boundary. In fact, even an internal clause boundary may not separate the


two, as demonstrated by the examples in (2):


(2) a. *My uncle is not generous, but [I love himself anyway].


b. Mary suspects that [John admires himself/*herself too much].


c. Mary waited for [John to excuse himself/*herselfj.


d. I told you that [Mary would blame *myself7*yourself /herself].


**82** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-82-full.png)

www.am oriteaching.co.kr^-.:'


In other words, a reflexive and its antecedent must be elements of the same simple


clause; the smallest clause which contains either one of them must also contain the other.


For this reason, we say that the relationship between a reflexive and its antecedent in


English in clause-bounded.


**2. Antecedents of Regular Pronouns**


Non-reflexive pronouns must also agree with their antecedents in person, number, and


gender. However, unlike reflexives, “regular” pronouns cannot take an antecedent within


their same minimal clause. The examples in (3) and (4) show that the antecedent of a


regular pronoun cannot be the subject or object of the same simple sentence. (The “#”


sign is used here to indicate that a pronoun cannot refer to the same person as a preceding


NP, as explained in the notes following each sentence.)


(3) a. My brother admires #him. _**{him**_ must refer to someone else)


b. My sister admires #her. _**{her**_ must refer to someone else)


c. My parents admire #them. _**{them**_ must refer to someone else)


(4) a. John described Mary to #him/#her.


(pronoun cannot refer to John or Mary)


b. John wrote Mary a poem about #him/#her.


(pronoun cannot refer to John or Mary)


As the examples in (5) demonstrate, regular pronouns may take an antecedent within


the same sentence provided it is not part of the same minimal clause. In other words,


there must be a clause boundary between the pronoun and its antecedent.


(5) a. My uncle is not generous, but [I love him anyway].


_**{him**_ can refer to ‘my uncle’)


b. Mary suspects that [John admires her/#him too much].


_**{her**_ can refer to Mary, but _**him**_ cannot refer to John)


c. Mary waited for [John to excuse #him/her],


_**{her**_ can refer to Mary, but _**him**_ cannot refer to John)


Chapter 3 English Syntax • **83**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-83-full.png)

CEE©


What English Language Teachers Should Know


d. I told you that [Mary would be blame me/you/#her].


_**(her**_ eannot refer to Mary)


When we compare these examples with the parallel reflexive examples in (1-2), we


see that reflexive and non-reflexive pronouns appear to be in complementary distribution:


if the reflexive form is grammatical, then the corresponding regular pronoun could not


be used to refer to the same antecedent, and vice versa.


(6) Reflexive binding:


a. Reflexive pronouns must agree with their antecedents in person, number, and


gender.


b. A reflexive pronoun must find an antecedent with its minimal clause.


(7) Pronoun binding:


a. Non-reflexive pronouns must agree with their antecedents in person, number,


and gender.


b. A non-reflexive pronoun may not take as its antecedent an NP which occurs


within its minimal clause.

**Complementizers**


**1. Appositive that-clauses and relative clauses**


The use of _**that**_ as both a relative pronoun and a pure complementizer can lead to


confusion between appositive _**that-clauses**_ and relative clauses:


(1) The suggestion that he might fail is disturbing.


(2) The suggestion that he made is disturbing.


**84**  - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-84-full.png)

lllll
www.amoriteaching.co.kr^vr

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-84-0.png)


the suggestion that he made

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-84-1.png)


In (1) is a clause functioning as a complement of N (equivalent to a direct object


of the verb _**suggest**_ : Someone suggested that he might fail). The complementizer _**that**_


has no function in its own clause, and the embedded clause is complete without _**that**_


and can stand alone as a complete sentence _**He might fail.**_ The //raZ-clause can also


function as an NP: _**That he might fail is disturbing.**_ In (2) is a clause functioning as


a modifier of N. The relative _**that**_ has the function of direct object in its own clause,


and hence the embedded clause is not complete without the relative: _***He made.**_ The


embedded clause cannot function as an NP: * _**That he made is disturbing.**_ Note that _**which**_


can substitute for _**that**_ in this case but not in the other: _**The suggestion which he made**_


_**is disturbing. *The suggestion which he might fail is disturbing.**_

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-85-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


**2. English complementizers: whether, if**


The item whieh might function as a complementizer in English is interrogative _**if**_


However, it might seem as if there is a potential parallelism between /'/'and interrogative


wh-adverbs like _**when/whereAvhether,**_ since they appear to occupy the same position in


sentences like:


(3) 1 don’t know _**[where/when/whether/if**_ he will go]


Hence we might be tempted to analyse _**if**_ as an interrogative adverb.


However, there are a number of reasons for rejecting this possibility. For one thing,


//'differs from interrogative adverbs like _**where/when/whether**_ not only in its form (it isn’t


a >v//-word, as we can see from the fact that it doesn’t begin with _**wit),**_ but also in the


range of syntactic positions it can occupy. For example, whereas typical wh-adverbs can


occur in finite and infinitive clauses alike, the complementizer _**if**_ is restricted to


introducing finite clauses:


(4) 1 wonder _**[where/when/whether/if**_ I should go] [=finite clause]


(5) 1 wonder _**[where/when/whether/* if**_ to go] [^infinite clause]


Moreover, _**if**_ is different from interrogative wh-adverbs in that it cannot be used to


introduce a clause which serves as the complement of a preposition:


(6) I’m not certain about _**[whether/when/where**_ he’ll go]


(7) *l’m concerned over [/'/'taxes are going to be increased]


(8) *I’m puzzled at _**[that**_ he should have resigned]


(9) *l’m not very keen on _**[for**_ you to go there]


Finally, whereas a wh-adverb can typically be coordinated with (e.g. joined by a


coordinating conjunction like _**and/or)**_ the adverb _**not,**_ this is not true of _**if**_


(10) 1 don’t know _**[whether**_ or _**not**_ he’ll turn up]


(11) *1 don’t know [//'or _**not**_ he’ll turn up]


**86** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-86-full.png)

www.am oriteaching.co.kr^^c


For reasons such as these, it seems more appropriate to categorise _**if**_ as an interrogative


complementizer, and _**whether/where/when**_ as interrogative adverbs.


**3. English complementizers: whether, if, that, for**


Wh-complements can be either tensed or infinitival, and are typically selected by verbs


such as _**wonder, know,**_ and _**ask.**_ Because of their interrogative form, they are sometimes


referred to as _**indirect questions.**_ Some examples of such complements are given in (1).


(1) a. I wonder[why Garth Brooks likes singing at the fair].

S


b. The fans asked [which star would be singing first].


S


c. Trisha Yearwood doesn’t know [which song to sing],

S


d. Garth Brooks decided [where to put his guitar].


S


The clausal complements in (la-b) are tensed, and the ones in (lc-d) are infinitival.


There do exist two possible WH-complementizers, namely _**whether**_ and _**if,**_ that occur


in WH-complements, as in (2).


(2) I wonder [if/whether Garth Brooks likes singing at the fair].


S


_**Whether**_ and _**if**_ in (2) do not appear in their positions as a result of WFI-Movement;


there is no obvious position in the clause they could have originated in and been moved


from. Rather, they appear to originate in “complementizer position,” just like _**that**_ and


_**for.**_ Because we interpret them as WFI-words, we consider the clauses they introduce


WH-complements, distinct from other types of tensed or infinitival complements. This


distinction is further supported by evidence that neither _**whether**_ nor _**if**_ can introduce


tensed or infinitival complements of verbs that do _**not**_ select indirect questions, such as


_**think**_ and _**prefer.**_


Chapter 3 English Syntax • **87**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-87-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


(3) a. *1 think [whether Garth Brooks likes singing at the fair].

S


b. *1 prefer [if Garth Brooks likes singing at the fair].

S


Similarly, neither _**that**_ nor _**for**_ can introduce indirect questions, as we see in (4), where


these complementizers are ungrammatical in complements selected by _**wotuler,**_ a verb that


always selects a WH-complement.


(4) a. *1 wonder [that Garth Brooks like singing at the fair].

S


b. *1 wonder [for Trisha to sing an encore].

S


We can see from the above discussion and examples that verbs clearly _**select**_ not only


the category of their complement, namely S, but also the complementizer that introduces


it.


Finally, observe that _**whether**_ and _**if**_ are distinct from each other with respect to the


kinds of WH-complements they introduce. As we see in (5), _**whether**_ introduces both


tensed and infinitival WH-complements.


(5) a. I wonder whether John left.


b. I wonder whether to leave.


The sentences in (6) show, in contrast, that //introduces only tensed WH-complements.


(6) a. I wonder if John left.


b. *1 wonder if (John) to leave.


Another interesting point about WH-complementizers is that they are not optional, as


we see by the ungrammaticality of the sentences in (7-8). _**Whether**_ and _**if**_ thus contrast


with the optional English complementizers _**that**_ and _**for.**_


(7) a. I wonder whether Garth Brooks likes singing at the fair.


b. *1 wonder Garth Brooks like singing at the fair.


**88** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-88-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


(8) a. I wonder if John left,


b. *1 wonder John left.


The above data suggest that English complementizers _**that, for if,**_ and _**whether**_ have


very distinct properties regarding the types of clausal complements they introduce. _**That**_


introduces tensed, non-WH-complements, and _**for,**_ infinitival, non-WH-complements.


_**Whether**_ and _**if**_ on the other hand, introduce _**only**_ WM-complements; furthermore, _**whether**_


introduces either tensed or infinitival WH-complements, but _**if**_ only tensed


WH-complements. Finally, both _**that**_ and _**for**_ are optional in English, but _**whether**_ and


_**if**_ are obligatory in indirect questions without WH-movement.


Verbs therefore appear to select the clauses introduced by certain complementizers.


The distribution of complementizers in English is represented in table below.


+WH -WH


Tensed complements whether/if (obligatory) that (optional)
Infinitival complements whether (obligatory) for (optional)

**Case Theory**
V ________________ . ... .. ..


**(1) Case-Filter and Adjacency Requirement**


It is well known that subject NPs of finite clauses get Nominative Case, and the NPs


that are the complements of a preposition or a verb bear Accusative Case. In addition,


the NP that is the subject of /^-infinitive clause introduced by a prepositional


complementizer _**for**_ carries Accusative Case. The sentences in (1) show Case-related


phenomena. 1


(1) a. _**He**_ often helps _**her.**_


b. _**It’**_ s surprising for _**them**_ to talk to _**me**_ like that.


Chapter 3 English Syntax * 8 9


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-89-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


In fact, the case requirement on NPs is obligatory in that when an NP fails to have


Case, it results in ungrammaticality. In generative grammar, this is accounted for by the


Case Filter and Adjacency Requirement:


Case Filter


Every o v e rt NP m u s t be assigned Case by such a p p ro p ria te Case assignors as a


p re p o s itio n, th e fin ite te n se, c o m p le m e n tiz e r _**for,**_ and a tra n s itiv e verb .


Adjacency Requirement


For th e Case assig n o r to assign Case to NPs, th e tw o m u st be a d ja ce n t.


Consider the following examples:


(2) a. Poirot speaks [ np English] fluently.


b. *Poirot speaks fluently [ np English].


c. Poirot sincerely believes [ ip English to be important].


d. *Poirot believes sincerely [ ip English to be important].


e. Poirot believes sincerely [cp that English is important].


In (2a) the verb _**speak**_ takes an NP complement _**English**_ and VP further includes an


adjunct _**fluently.**_ The NP _**Poirot**_ is case-marked by the finite INFL; the NP _**English**_ is


case-marked by the transitive verb. In (2b) the constituents of the sentence are not altered


and yet the sentence is ungrammatical. The only contrast with (2a) is that the V _**speak**_


and the complement NP _**English**_ are no longer next to each other or adjacent.


A similar pattern is found in (2c) and (2d). In both sentences _**believe**_ takes an IP


complement. In (2c) the verb _**believe**_ case-marks the subject NP of the lower clause


_**(.English**_ ) and the sentence is grammatical, while in (2d) the non-adjacency of the verb


and the NP to which it should assign structural case leads to ungrammaticality.


The adjacency requirement has nothing to say about (2e). On the one hand, a finite


clause does not need to be case-marked. The case filter applies to NPs, not to clauses.


9 0 • What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-90-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


On the other hand, the subject of the finite clause, the NP _**English**_, will satisfy the case


filter because it receives nominative from the finite I.


Consider the following examples:


(3) a. I wonder whether [n> _**ec**_ to invite Thelma].


b. *1 wonder whether [n> I/me to invite Thelma].


c. *1 wonder whether [n> Louise to invite Thelma].


In (3b) and (3c) the subject of the non-finite clause is overt. The sentences are


ungrammatical because the subject NP cannot be assigned case. _**Whether**_ is a


complementizer, but unlike _**for,**_ it is not a preposition, i.e. it is not a case-assigner. Hence,


the subject position of the non-finite IP lacks case. This means that the only way to render


the sentence grammatical is to insert a non-overt NP in the subject position: (3a) with


a non-overt subject is grammatical. If we say that the Case-Filter applies only to overt


NPs, then the grammaticality of (3a) follows.


Consider the following examples:


(4) a. I know [ ip John to be the best candidate].


b. I don’t know [cp whether [ ip ___ to go to the party]].


c. *1 don’t know [cp whether [ ip John to go to the party]].


_**Know**_ takes an IP complement. In (4b), the presence of _**whether**_ indicates that we


have an infinitival clause of the type CP. In this example, there is no overt subject in


the infinitival clause, thus the case filter does not come into play with respect to the


subject NP of the lower clause. In (4c) _**know**_ again takes a clausal CP complement


(witness the presence of _**whether).**_ In this example the infinitival clause contains an overt


NP subject _**John**_ . The sentence is ungrammatical because it violates the case filter.


Infinitival _**to**_ is assumed to be unable to assign case. The potential case assigner _**know**_


is separated from the relevant NP by the maximal projection CP, which is a barrier.

What English Language Teachers Should Know


**(2) Adjectives and Nouns**


Nouns and adjectives are not case assigners in English:


(5) a. Poirot envies Miss Marple.


b. *Poirot is envious Miss Marple.


c. Poirot is envious of Miss Marple.


d. *Poirot’s envy Miss Marple


e. Poirot’s envy of Miss Marple


All the examples in (5) contain a main predicate morphologically and semantically


related to the verb _**envy.**_ In (5a) _**envy,**_ the verb, is used; in (5b) and (5c) we find the


related adjective _**envious;**_ in (5d) and (5e) the noun _**envy.**_


In (5a) case assignment is straightforward: _**Poirot**_ is assigned nominative by the finite


inflection and _**Miss Marple**_ is assigned accusative by the transitive verb _**envy.**_


(5b) is ungrammatical. If we compare it with the grammatical (5a) the only difference


is that we have replaced the verb _**envy**_ by the adjective _**envious.**_ Apparently (5b) can


be rescued by the insertion of the preposition _**of**_ as seen in (5c).


Let us turn to (5d) and (5e). First of all, we see that these NPs contain a genitive


NP, _**Poirot's,**_ in front of their head N. Let us assume that there is an element POSS in


the specifier position of NPs which is able to assign genitive to the NP in that position.

**01** **Syntactic Ambiguity**


The following English sentences are all ambiguous. Provide a paraphrase (a sentence with

roughly the same meaning) for each of the possible meanings.


a. John said Mary went to the store quickly.


b. I discovered an old English poem.


c. Dick finally decided on the boat.


d. The professor’s appointment was shocking.


e. The design has big squares and circles.


f. That sheepdog is too hairy to eat.


a.


i ) John said quickly that Mary went to the store,


ii) John said that Mary went quickly to the store,


b.


i ) l discovered a poem written in Old English.


ii) 1 discovered an old poem written in Modern English.


c.


i ) Dick finally chose the boat.


ii) Dick finally decided when he was on the boat.


d.


i ) It was shocking that the professor was appointed.


ii) The appointment made by the professor was shocking.


e.


i ) The design has big squares and big circles.


ii) The design has squares that are big and (big and/or small) circles,


f.


i ) That sheepdog is loo hairy to eat anything,


ii) That sheepdog is too hairy for someone to eat.

**02 Interpretation of Pronouns**


Recall that a personal pronoun can’t have an antecedent in the same clause and that a

reflexive pronoun _**must**_ have an antecedent in the same clause.


Erma loves her. _**(Her**_ cannot refer to _**Erma.)**_


Erma loves herself _**(Herself**_ must refer to _**Erma.)**_


Now, consider the following sentences. What are the possible antecedents of her and

herself, and how does this evidence complicate our rules of pronoun reference? Think


about the structure of the sentence, in particular the structure of the phrase Erma’s mother.


Erma’s mother loves her.


Erma’s mother loves herself.


_Erma_ can be the antecedent of _her,_ which appears to violate the restriction that pronouns refer to an


antecedent outside of the clause that contains them. But, the difference here seems to be that _Erma_


is contained inside the subject NP (and therefore for some reason doesn't “count” as the subject NP).


Syntactic accounts of this phenomenon make use of c-command; the NP _Erma_ is more deeply embedded


in the NP - far enough away to allow conference. Erma’s mother is the antecedent of _herself_ here


the whole NP is the antecedent, not just the “inner” part of it.

_the door_ must remain with the NP _the key_ in order to yield a grammatical passive.

They bought it.

They bought it at that castle.


*Thcy bought it.

The book on that castle was bought (by them).

**07** **Verb Complementation**


Consider the following minimal pair of sentences:

On the surface, (a) and (b) above look alike, that is, they have the sequences


of[vpV-NP-to-Inf|. They have different structures, however. Discuss as many structural

differences between the two as possible.

b. John wants for him to study hard.

### **English Grammar**

**SMoun and Determiners**


**1. Determiners**


**(D Categories of determiners**


Determiners are words which specify the range of reference of a noun, e.g. by making


it definite (the book), indefinite (a book), or by indicating quantity (many books).


pre-determiners central determiners post-determiners



all, both, half Articles:



double, twice, one-third, etc.


what, such, etc.



the, a, an


Demonstratives:


this, these, that, those


**Possessives:**


my, your, his, her, etc.


Quantifiers:


some, any, no, every, each,


either, neither, enough, much


Wh-detcmiiners:


what(ever), which(ever)


whose



**Cardinal numbers:**


one, two, three, four, etc.


**Ordinal numbers:**


first, second, third, etc.


**General ordinals:**


next, last, other, etc.


**Quantifiers:**


many, few, little, several,


more, less, etc.



(1) Through _**all these**_ years she had avoided the limelight.


(2) The party needs _**double that**_ number of votes to win the election.


(3) They had no knowledge of _**such a**_ letter.


(4) This was Johnson s best match in _**the last two**_ years.


Chapter 4 English Grammar • 101


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-101-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


**(2) Sequences of determiners**


Sometimes more than one determiner occurs before a noun. Their ordering is important,


and not all sequences are possible, for example,


(1) _**Both my**_ feet feel cold.


*My both feet feel cold.


(2) _**All the**_ examples in this book are clear.


*The all examples in this book are clear.


(3) I like _**all the three**_ boys.


*1 like the all three boys.


**2. Articles**


**(1) The articles in generic reference**


Generic reference refers to what is general or typical for whole class of objects. All


three types of article can be used to make a generic reference: _**the**_ usually, and _**a/an**_


always, with singular count nouns; zero with plural count nouns and with noncount nouns.


For example:


(1) _**The car**_ became an increasing necessity of life in the twentieth century.


(2) _**A car**_ became an increasing necessity of life in the twentieth century.


(3) _**Cars**_ became an increasing necessity of life in the twentieth century.


(4) _**Velvet**_ is an excellent material for curtains.


**(2) The articles in specific reference**


The article _**the**_ marks a noun phrase as _**definite**_ : that is, as referring to something


which can be identified uniquely in the contextual or general knowledge shared by speaker


and hearer. Such shared knowledge is partly a knowledge of the world and partly a


knowledge of English grammar.


Where the use of _**the**_ depends on shared knowledge of the world, we may speak of


_**situational reference.**_


**102** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-102-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


(1) Do you see _**the**_ bird sitting on _**the**_ lower branch?


(2) Oli dear! _**The**_ stain hasn’t come out of _**the**_ carpet.


In such cases, the identity of the particular bird, branch, stain, and carpet is obvious


because they arc physically present and visible.


The use of _**the**_ may be determined by _**grammatical**_ factors. _**Grammatical**_ determination


is of two kinds:


(a) _**Anaphoric**_ reference, where _**the**_ indicates identity of reference with that established


earlier in the discourse:


(1) Fred bought a radio and a video-recorder, but he returned _**the**_ radio.


(2) When she tried to open her front door, she couldn’t get _**the**_ key into _**the**_ lock.


In (1) the anaphora is direct: but in (2) anaphoric reference may be indirect, requiring


some support from general knowledge. Flere the two definite articles are correctly


interpreted as grammatically anaphoric only because we know that a front door has a


lock, and opening one involves using a key.


(b) _**Cataphoric**_ reference, where _**the**_ indicates that the identity of the reference will


be established by what follows:


(1) I am trying to find _**the**_ book that I wanted to show you.


(2) How did you get _**the**_ (=that) mud on your coat?


In (1) _**the**_ is only justified by the addressee knowing that the speaker had planned


to show him or her a book. In (2) there is the presumption that the addressee knows


there is mud on the coat.


**3. Genitives**


**(D The Uses of Genitive**


The meanings expressed by the genitive can conveniently be shown through


paraphrase; at the same time, we can compare the analogous use of the ^/•construction.

Mrs Johnson owns this coat.


The ship has a funnel.

The heart contains two ventricles.

X released the prisoner.


The letter is from Mother.


The cheeses were produced in England.


The shoes are designed for children.


The degree is a doctorate.



Sometimes a genitive can be ambiguous. A phrase like _**Sylvia s photograph**_ is


ambiguous in three ways; _**Sylvia’s**_ might be possessive genitive (she owns the photo),


a subjective genitive (she took the photo), or an objective genitive (someone photographed


her). The sentence _**Liz had heard about Jane’s betrayal**_ has two potential meanings. **If**


Jane betrayed someone, the genitive is a subjective genitive; if someone betrayed Jane,


1 0 4 • What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-104-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


the genitive is an objective genitive. The sentence can be “disambiguated” if the genitive


construction also includes a prepositional phrase that acts as a patient, as in _**Liz had heard**_


_**about Jane’s betrayal of her friends.**_ This is a clear example of how the grammar of


a structure and its semantics can diverge.


Grammatically _**Jane’s betrayal of her friends**_ is an NP containing the noun head


_**betrayal**_ which is modified by the genitive _**Jane’s**_ and the prepositional phrase _**of her**_


_**friends.**_ Semantically, however, _**Jane(’s)**_ is acting like an agent subject, _**betrayal**_ is acting


like a verb, and _**of her friends is**_ a patient direct object. Similarly, if Jane is the one


being betrayed, the sentence can be “disambiguated” by including an agent - _**Liz had**_


_**heard about Jane’s betrayal by her boss.**_


**(2) Double Genitive**


An of-construction can be combined with an s-genitive or possessive pronoun into


a ‘double’ genitive:


(1) Shannon is a friend of _**my wife’s.**_


(2) This writer’s style is no favourite _**of mine.**_


The noun in the genitive must be both definite and personal. Unlike the simple


genitive, the double genitive usually implies that the meaning is not unique, i.e. that ‘my


wife has several friends’. Compare:


(3) He is _**Leda’s brother.**_


(suggests Leda has one, or more than one, brother)


(4) He is _**a brother of Leda’s.**_


(suggests Leda has more than one brother)


Chapter 4 English Grammar • 105


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-105-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


_**m**_


**Pronouns**


**1. The Interpretation of Pronouns**


Reflexive pronouns (the _**-self**_ pronouns) and personal pronouns _**{he/him, she/her, it,**_


_**we/us,**_ etc) differ in an interesting way with respect to the syntactic position of their


linguistic antecedents. To illustrate, consider the following set of sentence:


John like him. _**{Him**_ cannot refer to John.)


John likes himself. _**{Himself**_ must refer to John.)


These data illustrate that although _**himself**_ requires an antecedent in the same clause,


the opposite is true of _**him,**_ the antecedent of this pronoun _**cannot**_ be in the same clause.


Consider the following examples:


Elmer thinks that Irma must know him.


    - Elmer thinks that Irma must know _**himself**_


In the first sentence, _**him**_ occurs in a subordinate clause (the underlined section). _**Him**_


can refer to _**Elmer**_ because in this case _**Elmer**_ is outside of the clause containing _**him.**_


The second sentence shows that the reflexive pronoun _**himself**_ on the other hand, can’t


take _**Elmer**_ as an antecedent, because _**Elmer**_ is outside the (underlined) clause containing


the reflexive. This evidence again suggests not only that we are very much aware of clause


boundaries in general but also that we can easily (unconsciously, at least) identify


subordinate clauses. We can informally state the rules of pronoun reference, which depend


on our understanding of clause boundaries, as follows:


Principle A:


A reflexive pronoun must have an antecedent _inside_ of the clause that contains it.


Principle B:


A personal pronoun must have an antecedent _outside_ of the clause that contains it.


**106** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-106-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.krV](http://www.amoriteaching.co.krV)


**2. Reflexive Pronouns**


The basic reflexive pronoun takes the function of a noun phrase in the structure of


the clause or phrase: it may be an object, a complement, or a prepositional complement.


Since it corefers to the subject, however, the reflexive pronoun cannot itself be a subject.


In this, it shows that it belongs to ‘object territory’, and has affinities with the objective


personal pronoun. But it contrasts with the objective pronoun in meaning in examples


such as this:


(1) He saw _**himself/him**_ in the mirror.


In (1), _**himself**_ is necessarily preferential to the subject _**he,**_ while _**him**_ necessarily refers


to some other person. We have to use the reflexive rather than the ordinary objective


pronoun. The reflexive pronoun must agree with the subject in terms of gender, number,


and person:


She saw _**herself/*himself**_ in the mirror.


For an object, complement, or prepositional object which is preferential with the


subject, the reflexive pronoun is obligatory.


The basic reflexive pronoun always prefers to the subject of its own clause, even


though that subject may be ‘understood’. For example, the implied subject of the _**-ing**_


participle clause in (2) is _**Vincent**_, and _**himself**_ is therefore the appropriate reflexive


pronoun:


(2) Freeing _**himself**_ with a sharp knife, _**Vincent**_ lurched towards the door.


In (3), it is _**John**_ who is understood to be the subject of the infinitive clause, and


the appropriate reflexive is therefore _**himself**_ even though the subject of the main clause


is _**she:**_


(3) She asked _**John**_ to invite _**himself/*herself**_

**(1) Obligatory Reflexive Pronoun as Object**


We may distinguish the following types of construction in which the reflexive pronoun,


when preferential with the subject, is always used in preference to the objective case


pronoun:


(a) With reflexive verbs, _**ie**_ verbs which always require a reflexive object, such as


_**pride oneself on:**_


_**She**_ always _**prides herself/*prides**_ on her academic background.


Examples of other reflexive verbs



_**absent oneself (from)**_


_**demean oneself**_ <formal>


_**perjure oneself**_



_**avail oneself (of**_


_**ingratiate oneself (with)**_



(b) With semi-reflexive verbs, _**ie**_ verbs where the reflexive pronoun may be omitted


with little or no change of meaning, such as _**behave (oneself, shave (oneself:**_


_**Behave yourself/Behave**_ now!


_**He**_ has to _**shave himself/shave**_ twice a day.


Other semi-reflexive verbs are:


_**adjust (oneself) to dress (oneself**_


_**hide (oneself**_ _**identify (oneself with**_


_**prepare (oneself) for**_ _**prove (oneself (to be)**_


_**wash (oneself)**_ _**worry (oneself)**_


**(2) Obligatory Reflexive Pronoun after a Preposition**


The following types of construction require a reflexive pronoun after the preposition,


if the subject is the intended antecedent:


(a) With prepositional objects, _**ie**_ prepositional complements which have a close


connection with the preceding verb:

**(3) Optional Reflexive Pronoun**


The basic reflexive pronoun is sometimes optional, in the sense that it may acceptably


be replaced by the more usual ordinary objective pronoun. The _**self-**_ forms are chosen


to supply special emphasis:


(a) In some spatial prepositional phrases:


Mason stepped back, gently closed the door _**behind him(self),**_ and walked down


the corridor.


They left the apartment, pulling the spring lock shut _**behind them(selves).**_


(b) In ‘semi-emphatic’ use. The constructions in which the ‘semi-emphatic’ reflexive


occurs are the following:


( i ) After the prepositions _**like, than, (as...) as, but (for), except (for),**_ and _**as**_


_**for:**_


For someone _**like me/myself**_ this is a big surprise.


_**Except for us/ourselves,**_ the whole village was asleep.


According to the manager, no one works _**as**_ hard _**as him(self).**_

( ii) When a reflexive pronoun (particularly a 1st person pronoun) is coordinated


with another phrase:


They have never invited _**Margaret and me/myself**_ to dinner.

**3. Reflexive and Reciprocal Pronouns**


Reflexive and reciprocal pronouns replace NP objects that have the same referent as


the subject of the sentence. The coreference must be within the clause; thus we have


a contrast between


(1) Penelope begged Jane to look after _**her.**_ (=Penelope)


Penelope begged Jane to look after _**herself.**_ (=Jane)


(2) The men/, will shoot the arrows/ at _**each otherj.**_ (=the arrows)


The men/ will shoot the arrows/ at _**each otheri.**_ (=the men)


(3) John and Mary despise themselves.


(= John despises John, and Mary despises Mary.)


John and Mary despise each other.


(= John despises Mary and Mary despises John.)


_U._ **Relative Pronoun Clauses**


**(D Restrictive versus Nonrestrictive Relative Clauses**


A restrictive relative clause is one that serves to restrict the reference of the noun


phrase modified. In (1), the restrictive relative clause _**who lives in Canada**_ restricts _**my**_


_**sister**_ by specifying the sister in Canada. The sentence implies that the speaker has more


than one sister, but only one sister in Canada is a biologist. It could be an answer to


the question _**Which of your sisters is a biologist?**_ The information added by the relative


clause identifies the sister. 1


(1) My sister _**who lives in Canada**_ is a biologist.


1 1 0 • What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-110-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr) _p_


Sentence (2) contains a nonrestrictive relative clause, indicated as such by the commas


around it. A _**nonrestrictive relative clause**_ adds information about the noun modified. The


noun’s reference is already clear, the clause does not restrict it. Thus, in (2) the relative


clause is just an added comment to the main clause content _**my sister is a biologist.**_ The


relative clause in essence says “Oh, by the way, she lives in Canada.” There is no


implication that the speaker has other sisters.


(2) My sister, _**who lives in Canada,**_ is a biologist.


**(2) Form Criteria Distinguishing Nonrestrictive and Restrictive Relative Clauses**


Several form criteria distinguish nonrestrictive relative clauses from their restrictive


counterparts.


© Punctuation


Nonrestrictive relative clauses have commas around them, as in (3a). Restrictive


relative clauses must not be separated by commas, as shown in (3b).


(3) a. My sister, who lives in Canada, is a biologist,


b. My sister who lives in Canada is a biologist.


© Modification of proper nouns


Nonrestrictive relative clauses can modify proper nouns, as in (4a); restrictive


relatives, as in (4b), cannot.


(4) a. John, who is a linguist, was not impressed by Professor Fish’s arguments,


b. *John who is a linguist was not impressed by Professor Fish’s arguments.


© Modification of _**any, every, no,**_ etc.


Nonrestrictive relative clauses may not modify _**any, every,**_ or _**no**_ + noun or


indefinite pronouns such as _**anyone, everyone,**_ or _**no one,**_ as shown by (5a);


restrictive relatives may, as shown in (5b).


(5) a. *Any man, who goes back on his word, is no friend of mine,


b. Any man who goes back on his word is no friend of mine.


Chapter/* English Grammar * 1 1 1


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-111-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


@ _**That**_ as relative pronoun


Nonrestrictive relative clauses may not be introduced by _**that,**_ as shown by (6a);


restrictive relatives may, as in (6b).


(6) a. *The plan, that we discussed yesterday, will be adopted,


b. The plan that we discussed yesterday will be adopted.


(5) Stacking


Nonrestrictive relative clauses cannot be stacked. Stacking results in ungrammatical


sentences like (7a). Restrictive relatives can be stacked, as in (7b).


(7) a. *They gave the job to Rob, who is very qualified, who starts next month,


b. I really like that car that you have that your wife is always zipping around


town in.


(6) Sentence modification


Nonrestrictive relative clauses may modify an entire sentence, that is, a preceding


independent clauses, as in (8a). Restrictive relatives like (8b) may only modify


noun phrases.


(8) a. Professor Fish gave everyone an A, which was just fine with Alice,


b. *Professor Fish gave everyone an A which was just fine with Alice.

**Adverbs**
_**j**_

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-111-0.png)


**1. Stative and Dynamic Adjectives**


Adjectives are characteristically stative. Many adjectives, however, can be seen as


dynamic. In particular, most adjectives that are susceptible to subjective measurement are


capable of being dynamic. Stative and dynamic adjectives differ syntactically in a number


of ways. For example, a stative adjective such as _**tall**_ cannot be used with the progressive


aspect or with the imperative:


1 1 2 • What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-112-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


*He’s being _**tall.**_ *Be _**tall.**_


On the other hand, we can use _**careful**_ as a dynamic adjective:


He’s being _**careful.**_ Be _**careful.**_


A general semantic feature of dynamic adjectives seems to be that they denote qualities


that are thought to be subject to control by the possessor and hence can be restricted


temporally.


Adjectives that can be used dynamically include:


brave, calm, cheerful, conceited, cruel, foolish, friendly, funny, good, greedy,


helpful, jealous, naughty, noisy, tidy, etc.


**2. Unmarked and Marked Adjectives**


When we want to state the age of a person, we will normally use the expression _**x**_


_**years old,**_ for example:


Mr Jespersen is 75 _**years old.**_


We will not say:


*Mr Jespersen is 75 _**years young.**_


However, the choice of adjective here has nothing to do with the number of years,


and we would use the same expression for somebody very young, for example:


His granddaughter is two years _**old/*young.**_


Similarly:


The water is two meters _**deep/*shallow.**_


Susan is only five feet _**tall/*short.**_


‘Measure’ adjectives like _**old, deep**_, and _**tall**_ cover a scale of measurement. Such


adjectives have two terms for the opposite ranges of the scale ( _**old/young, deep/shallow,**_


_**tall/short**_ ), but use the upper range as the ‘unmarked’ term in measure expressions _**(old,**_


Chapter _U_ English Grammar • **113**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-113-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


_**deep, tall).**_ The ‘measure’ adjeelives which are used in this way include the following


set with the marked term give in parenthesis:


_**big (small)**_ _**deep (shallow)**_ _**fast (slow)**_ _**hard (soft)**_


_**high (low)**_ _**long (short)**_ _**old (young)**_ _**tall (short)**_


_**thick (thin)**_ _**wide (narrow)**_


This set of ‘measure’ adjectives is used in the same way in /tow-questions and, again,


the use of the unmarked term does not assume that only the upper range is applicable:


A: _**How old**_ is your daughter? [‘What is her age?’]


B: She is _**ten years old.**_ [‘Her age is ten years.’]


There are also adjectives outside the set listed above which are used in //tm'-questions,


in the same way as the ‘measure’ adjectives. Thus, if we want to know the size of


something, we would normally use _**big**_ or _**large**_, rather than _**small**_ or _**little,**_ for example:


_**How big**_ is your computer?


Similarly, we use _**bright**_ rather than _**dim, strong**_ rather than _**weak, heavy**_ rather than


_**light, etc:**_


_**How strong**_ is the engine of this car?


_**How accurate**_ is your digital watch?


**3. Distinguishing Adjectival Participles from Verbal Participles**


Often the difference between the adjective and the participle is not clear-cut. The


verbal force of the participle is explicit for the _**-ing**_ form when a direct object is present.


Hence, the following _**-ing**_ forms are participles that constitute a verb phrase with the


preceding auxiliary:


(1) Her views were _**alarming**_ her audience.


(2) You are _**frightening**_ the children.


(3) They are _**insulting**_ us.


1 U - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-114-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


Similarly, the verbal force is explicit for the _**-ed**_ form when a Ay-agent phrase with


a personal agent is present, indicating the correspondence to the active form of the


sentence:


(1) The man was _**offended**_ by the policeman.


(2) He is _**appreciated**_ by his students.


(3) She was _**misunderstood**_ by her parents.


For both participle forms, premodification by the intensifier _**very**_ is an explicit


indication that the forms have achieved adjective status:


(1) Her views were very _**alarming.**_


(2) You are very _**frightening.**_


(3) The man was very _**offended.**_


We might therefore expect that the presence of _**very**_ together with an explicit indicator


of verbal force would produce an unacceptable sentence.


*His views were very _**alarming**_ his audience.


_**U.**_ **Adjectival Use of -ing and -ed Participles**


There is a distinction in English between pairs of adjectives such as _**boring/ bored,**_


_**exciting/ excited,**_ and _**shocking shocked.**_ These forms are normally used as in examples


(la, b, and c), but many learners produce versions of the type shown in (Id and e). 1


(1) a. The lesson was interesting.


b. The teacher was amusing.


c. The students were interested and amused.


d. 1 didn’t like the lesson. I was boring.


e. I am more interesting in English grammar.


Chapter _U_ English Grammar • **115**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-115-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


In examples such as (Id and e), learners may use the _**-ing**_ form of the adjective


_**(boring, interesting**_ ) when they actually mean the _**-ed**_ form _**(bored, interested).**_ But why,


they may ask, can’t they say, _**I am boring today?**_ The answer is, of course, that they


certainly can say that (it’s grammatically correct), but is it what they mean? The meaning


distinction is not too difficult to explain.


These adjectives are derived from verbs that express emotions or feelings. When we


talk about an emotion, we can focus on the source (i.e. who or what causes it) or on


the experiencer (i.e. who or what is affected by it). When you are talking about the source,


you use the _**-ing**_ form. If a book (or a lesson or a person) causes the emotion, then it


is _**boring, interesting,**_ or _**exciting.**_ When you are talking about the experiencer, you use


the _**-ed**_ form. If people experience the emotion, then they are _**bored, interested,**_ or _**excited.**_


The cause is _**boring**_, the experiencer is _**bored.**_


We can take these observations and summarize them, as shown in Box 1.1. We can


also use this opportunity to include a list (as a teaching resource) of several adjectives


that have this meaning distinction.


Summary B© k 1.1 Adjectives like _boring_ and _bored_


Source or cause is _-ing_ Experiencer or affected is _-ed_


Things or people are: People (mostly) are


_amazing, amusing, annoying,_ _amazed, amused, annoyed,_


_astonishing, bewildering,_ _astonished, bewildered,_


_boring, confusing, depressing,_ _bored, confused, depressed,_


_disappointing, disgusting,_ _disappointed, disgusted,_


_embarrassing, exciting,_ _embarrassed, excited_


_exhausting, fascinating,_ _exhausted, fascinated,_


_frightening, horrifying,_ _frightened, horrified,_


_interesting, intriguing,_ _interested, intrigued,_


_irritating, puzzling, satisfying,_ _irritated, puzzled, satisfied,_


_shocking, surprising,_ _shocked, surprised,_


_terrifying, tiring, worrying_ _terrified, tired, worried_

**5. Adjective Complementation**


We distinguish seven kinds of construction in which an adjective is followed by a


/ 0 -infinitive clause. They are exemplified in the following sentences, which are


superficially alike:


(1) Bob is _**splendid**_ to wait.


(2) Bob is _**slow**_ to react.


(3) Bob is _**sorry**_ to hear it.


(4) Bob is _**hesitant**_ to agree with you.


(5) Bob is _**hard**_ to convince.


(6) The food is _**ready**_ to eat.


(7) It is _**important**_ to be accurate.


In types (1-4) the subject of the main clause _**(Boh)**_ is also the subject of the infinitive


clause. We can therefore always have a direct object in the infinitive clause if its verb


is transitive. For example, if we replace intransitive _**wait**_ by transitive _**build**_ in (1), we


can have: _**Bob is splendid to build this house.**_


For type (5-7), on the other hand, the subject of the infinitive is unspecified, although


the context often makes clear which subject is intended. In these types it is possible to


insert a subject proceeded by _**for: eg**_ in type (6): _**The food is ready (for the children)**_


_**to eat.**_


Type (1) has an analogue in a construction involving extraposition: _**It is splendid of**_


_**Bob to wait.**_ Adjectives in this type are evaluative of human behaviour. They include


_**careful**_, _**careless, crazy, foolish, mad, nice, silly, wise, wrong.**_


In type (2), the sentence corresponds to one in which the adjective becomes an adverb,


while the infinitive becomes the finite verb:


Bob is _**slow**_ to react.


Bob reacts _**slowly.**_


Other adjectives in this type include _**quick, prompt.**_


Chapter 4 English Grammar **• 117**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-117-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


In type (3), the head of the adjective phrase is an emotive adjective, and the infinitive


clause expresses causation:


I’m _**sorry**_ to have kept you waiting.


[‘I’m _**sorry**_ because I have kept you waiting’]


I was _**excited**_ to be there.


[‘To be there _**excited**_ me’]


Other adjectives in this type include _**afraid, ashamed, disappointed, glad, happy,**_


_**interested, relieved, surprised, worried.**_


In type (4), the head of the adjective phrase expresses volition or a modal meaning


such as ability or possibility. Adjectives in this type include _**able, anxious, certain, eager,**_


_**inclined, keen, likely, ready, reluctant, sure, unable, willing.**_


In type (5), the subject of the sentence is identified with the unexpressed object of


the infinitive clause, which must therefore have a transitive verb; hence we could not


have _***Bob is hard to arrive.**_ There is an analogous construction in which the adjective


is complement to an infinitive clause acting as subject or extraposed subject:


_**To convince Bob is hard.**_


_**It is hard to convince Bob.**_


Other adjectives in this group include _**difficult, easy, impossible, nice, pleasant.**_


In type (6), too the subject of the main clause is identified with the object of the


infinitive clause. But unlike type (5), type (6) has no analogous construction with an


infinitive clause subject:


The food is ready (for you) to eat.


*To eat the food is ready.


We can generally omit the infinitive clause _**{The food is ready),**_ and we can substitute


a passive infinitive clause without change of meaning _**(The food is ready to be eaten).**_


Other adjectives in this type include _**available, free, soft.**_

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-118-full.png)

w w w .am oriteaching.co.kr^r'


In type (7) the infinitive clause is an extraposed subject:


To spray the trees every year is _**essential.**_


It is _**essential**_ (for you) to spray the trees every year.


Adjectives in this type express volition, modality, or emotion. They include _**fortunate,**_


_**important, possible, surprising, wrong.**_


**6. Subjuncts**


There are two main types. Those with _**narrow orientation**_ are chiefly related to the


predication or to a particular part of the predication. Those with _**wide orientation**_ relate


more to the sentence as a whole, but show their subjunct character in tending to achieve


this through a particular relationship with one of the clause elements, especially the


subject.


**(1) Wide Orientation**


**® Viewpoint Subjuncts**


The subjuncts which express a viewpoint are largely concerned with the semantic


concept of respect, and are characteristically placed at _**I.**_


_**Architecturally,**_ the plans represent a magnificent conception.


_**From a personal viewpoint,**_ he is likely to do well in this post.


_**Looked at politically,**_ the proposal seems dangerous.


_**Weatherwise,**_ the outlook is dismal.


( 2 ) **Courtesy Subjuncts**


A small number of adverbs in _**-ly,**_ along with _**please,**_ serve to convey a formulaic


tone of politeness to a sentence. They normally occur at _**M:**_


You are _**cordially**_ invited to take your places.


He asked if I would _**please**_ read his manuscript.


She _**kindly**_ offered me her seat.


Chapter 4 English Grammar • **119**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-119-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


**(2) Narrow Orientation**


**® Item Subjuncts**


Subject-orientation effects a characterization of the referent of the subject with


respect to the process or state denoted by the verb. Two groups can be


distinguished: [A] a general group, and [B] a volitional group.


Group [A]: General Group


_**Resentfully,**_ the workers have stood by their leaders.


[‘The workers have stood by their leaders and were resentful about it’ or - in


context - ‘... but were nonetheless resentful’]


_**With great pride,**_ he accepted the award. [‘He was very proud to accept ...’]


For once, they have _**frankly**_ admitted their mistakes. [‘For once they have been


frank and admitted their mistakes’]


_**Manfully,**_ they insisted that the situation was not too bad. [‘It was manful of them


to insist ...’]


She has _**consistently**_ overruled the lawyer’s objections. [‘She has been consistent


in overruling ...’]


_**Bitterly,**_ he buried his children. [‘He was bitter when he ...’]


_**Sadly,**_ she wandered through the library. [‘She was sad when she ...’]


_**With great unease,**_ they elected him as their leader. [‘They were very uneasy when


they ...’]


Group [B]: Volitional Group


Common volitional subject-oriented adverbials include:


_**deliberately,**_ _**(unintentionally, purposely,**_ _**reluctantly,**_ _**voluntarily,**_ _**will fully,**_


_**(un)willingly, without intention, on purpose, with reluctance;**_ for example:


The volitional subjuncts differ from the others in several respects:


( I ) Volitional subjuncts have in common that they express the subject’s intention


or willingness, or the reverse.


(II) Volitional subjuncts can often occur with copular verbs if ( i ) the adjective

as complement is being used dynamically, or ( ii) if the noun-phrase


complement implies activity, or (iii) if there is a space adjunct:


He is being foolish _**intentionally.**_


He is being a nuisance _**deliberately.**_


He was in London _**reluctantly**_ for his daughter’s wedding.


Contrast:


*He is wealthy/foolish _**intentionally.**_


*He is an adult _**deliberately.**_


Subject subjuncts cannot co-occur with a nonpersonal subject in an intransitive or


active-voice clause:


_*** Consistently,**_ the water kept boiling.


     - _**Reluctantly,**_ the avalanche destroyed the chalet.


However, in the passive form it is the agent (whether present or not) that must


not be nonpersonal:


The chalet was _**reluctantly**_ destroyed (by the developers)/*(by the avalanche).


The lawyer’s objections were _**consistently**_ overruled.


Passive sentences with personal subject and agent leave an adverbial equivocal:


John was _**willingly**_ sent to friends for the summer (by his mother).


[either ‘John was willing’ or ‘his mother was willing’]


( 2 ) **Emphasizes**


Emphasizes are subjuncts expressing the semantic role of _**modality**_ with a


reinforcing effect on the meaning of a sentence.


1 _**just/really/simply**_ can’t believe a word he says!

(3) **Intensifiers**


The intensifier subjuncts are broadly concerned with the semantic category of


_**degree,**_ indicating an increase or decrease of the intensity with which a predication


is expressed.


They _**fully**_ appreciate the problem.


He has _**completely**_ ignored my question.


She was _**badly**_ in need of help.


_**How (very much)**_ they must have suffered!


They have _**practically**_ forced him to resign.


In spite of his manners, I _**rather**_ like him.


She _**sort of**_ laughed at the idea.


I was _**only**_ joking.


**@ Focusing Subjuncts**


There are two types of focusing subjunct that can operate; one is _**restrictive,**_ the


other is _**additive.**_


I _**merely**_ wanted to know his name.


_**Only**_ her sister visited her in hospital.


Fred had _**also**_ invited his mother-in-law.


_**Even**_ on Sundays, my doctor is willing to come and see me.


**7. Disjuncts**


Disjuncts tell us something about the speaker’s attitude towards what is being said.


They provide comment on the whole sentence itself.


**(1) Style disjuncts**


Style disjuncts convey the speaker’s comment on the style and form of what is being


said and define in some way the conditions under which ‘authority’ is being assumed


for the statement.


The semantic roles of disjuncts fall under two main heads:


**122** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-122-full.png)

www.am oriteaching.co.kr^^r


(a) Manner and modality, thus involving items sueh as _**crudely, frankly, honestly,**_


_**truthfully; eg:**_


_**(To put it) briefly,**_ there is nothing 1 can do to help.


You can, _**in all honesty,**_ expect no further payments.


(b) Respect, thus involving items such as _**generally, literally, personally, strictly; eg:**_


_**Strictly (in terms of the rules),**_ She should have conceded the point to her


opponent.


I would not, _**(speaking) personally,**_ have taken offence at the remark.


_**From what he said,**_ the other driver was in the wrong.


**(2) Content disjuncts**


Content disjuncts make an observation on the actual content of an utterance and on


its truth conditions.


Comment on the content of an utterance may be of two kinds:


(a) relating to _**certainty;**_


(b) relating to _**evaluation.**_


Both can be expressed by a wide range of adverb phrases, by prepositional phrases


and - especially those in (a) - by clauses.


(a) _**Certainty.**_ These disjuncts comment on the truth value of what is said, firmly


endorsing it, expressing doubt, or posing contingencies such as conditions or


reasons. For example, beside the statement ‘The play was written by Francis


Beaumont’, we may have:


The play was _**undoubtedly/apparently/perhaps**_ written by Francis Beaumont.


(b) _**Evaluation.**_ These disjuncts express an attitude to an utterance by way of


evaluation. Some express a judgment on the utterance as a whole, including


its subject:


_**Wisely,**_ Mrs Jensen consulted her lawyer.


(‘Mrs Jensen was wise in consulting her lawyer.’)
