**1. Assimilation**


An example of an adjustment in connected speech is the process of assimilation,


during which a given sound (the assimilating sound) takes on the characteristics of a


neighboring sound (the conditioning sound). In English it occurs frequently, both within


words and between words; it by no means marks a speaker as inarticulate or nonstandard.


There are three types of assimilation in English: (1) _**progressive**_, (2) _**regressive**_, and (3)


_**caulescent.**_


**(1) Progressive assimilation**


In progressive assimilation the conditioning sound precedes and affects the following


sound. Examples of progressive assimilation in English are the regular plural /s/ vs. /z/


alternation, in which the final sound of the stem conditions the voiced or voiceless form


of the suffix. This type of assimilation also occurs in the regular past tense /t/ vs. /d/


alternation:


Conditioning sound —* Assimilated sound


-v ending


bags /basg —* z/


backs /bask —* s/


_**-d**_ ending


moved /muwv —* d/


fished /IT/ — _**M**_


For the plural _**-s**_ ending, the voiced /g/ of _**bags**_ conditions the voiced form of the


_**-s**_ ending, causing it to be pronounced /z/, whereas the voiceless /k/ of _**back**_ conditions


the /s/ pronunciation of the ending. Notice that the same type of conditioning occurs in


the _**-d**_ endings.


Chapter 5 English Phonology • **231**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-231-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


**(2) Regressive assimilation**


In English, regressive assimilation is more pervasive as a purely phonological process


than in progressive assimilation. In regressive assimilation, the assimilated sound precedes


and is affected by the conditioning sound. Examples of this type of phenomenon are the


words _**grandpa**_ (the /p/ causes the /nd/ to be articulated as /m/: grsempa/) and _**pancake**_


(the /k/ causes the /n/ to become /r)/: panjkeyk/).


Regressive assimilation occurs commonly in the periphrastic modals _**has/ have to**_


(when expressing obligation) and _**used to**_ (when expressing former habitual action):


have + to — “hafla”


/haev/ + /tuw/ —> /haefto/


has r to —► “hasta”


/htez/ + /tuw/ —> /haesto/


_**u**_ . _**V**_
used + to _**—***_ usta


/yuwzd/ + /tuw/ —> /yuwsto/


In these examples, the voiceless /t/ of _**to**_ is the conditioning sound that causes the


voiced /v/, /z/, and /d/ preceding it to assimilate and become voiceless /f/, /s/, and /t/:


Assimilated sound <— Conditioning sound


/haef <— to/


/haes — to/


/yuws — to/


Another clear example of regressive assimilation is reflected in the English spelling


system - namely, in the four allomorphic variants of the negative prefix. Note that the


unmarked allomorph _**in-**_ occurs in all cases except when the subsequent sound is a bilabial


or a liquid: _**indecent, inept, invalid.**_ In the case of _**im-,**_ the initial bilabial sound of the


root word causes the organs of speech to approach a position closer to that of the


conditioning sound, as in _**impossible**_ or _**immobile.**_ Similarly, with the liquids /l/ and /r/,


the negative prefix is conditioned or changed to //- and />- respectively, as in _**illogical**_


and _**irrational:**_

manner assimilation.

**(3) Coalescent assimilation**


The third type of assimilation, coalescent assimilation, is a type of reciprocal


assimilation or palatalization: The first sound and second sound in a sequence come


together and mutually condition the creation of a third sound with features from both


original sounds.


Sound A + Sound B


Sound C


This process occurs most frequently in English when final alveolar consonants such


as /s, z/ and /t, d/ or final alveolar consonant sequences such as /ts, dz/ are followed


by initial palatal /y/. They then become palatalized fricatives and affricates, respectively:

**2. Dissimilation**


The process of dissimilation occurs when adjacent sounds become more different from


each other (rather than more similar, as is the case with assimilation).


An example of easing pronunciation through dissimilation is found in some varieties


of English, where there is a fricative dissimilation rule. This rule applies to sequences


/fe/ and /se/, changing them to [ft] and [st]. Here the fricative /e/ becomes dissimilar


to the preceding fricative by becoming a stop. For example, the words _**fifth**_ and _**sixth**_


come to be pronounced as if they were spelled _**fifi**_ and _**sikst.**_


A classic example of the same kind of dissimilation occurred in Latin, and the results


of this process show up in the derivational morpheme /-ar/ in English. In Latin a


derivational suffix _**-alis**_ was added to nouns to form adjectives. When the suffix was added


to a noun that contained the liquid /!/, the suffix was changed to _**-aris;**_ that is, the liquid


_l\l_ was changed to the liquid _hi._ These words came into English as adjectives ending


in _**-al**_ or in its dissimulated form _**-ar,**_ as shown in the following examples:


Chapter 5 English Phonology • **235**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-235-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


-al -ar


anecdot-al angul-ar


annu-al annul-ar


ment-al column-ar


pen-al perpendicul-ar


spiritu-al simil-ar


ven-al vel-ar


All of the _**-ar**_ adjectives contain an /!/, and as _**columnar**_ illustrates, the _**l\l**_ need not


be the consonant directly preceding the dissimilated segment.


**3. Neutralization**


Particular processes that result in the cancellation of contrasts between phonological


units are often described by the term neutralization. That is, two or more units that


ordinarily contrast lose that contrast in certain environments.


One example is vowel reduction. Consider the vowels in the following pairs of words:


A B


/i/ compete [i] competition [a]


_**l\l**_ medicinal [i] medicine [9]


_**Id**_ maintain [e] maintenance [o]


/e/ telegraph [e] telegraphy [a]


/as/ analysis [as] analytic [9]


_**Id**_ solid [a] solidity [3]


/o/ phone [o] phonetic


/«/ Talmudic [0] Talmud M


In these cases the stress rules of English, taking into account the different suffixes


in the words, change the stress pattern of the word. The vowel that is stressed in one


form becomes reduced in a different form and is therefore pronounced as [o]. The


**236** - What English Language Teachers Should Know

phonemic representations of all of the root morphemes contain an unreduced vowel such

Another example is flapping. Pronounce the following words in as natural a way as


possible, listening to how you pronounce the /t/ in each word:


cottage pretty butter total


Peter attic putting city


If you arc a speaker of North American English, your pronunciation of the /t/ in a


word such as ‘putting sounds like the /d/ in ‘pudding’ and these two words are pronounced


in exactly the same way. The sound that you are using is not the same as the /d/ sound


in a word such as ‘down’, however. While it is a voiced sound and is articulated at the


tooth ridge like a /d/, it is much shorter than a /d/. The tongue touches the tooth ridge


and is quickly pulled back. This sound is called a _**flap**_ and its occurrence is one of the


major differences between North American and British English. The phonetic symbol we


use for a llap is /r/.


When /t/ is pronounced as a flap, it is a positional variant of the /t/ sound. It only


occurs between vowels when the preceding vowel is stressed. Compare the pronunciation


of the words below, which contain aspirated and flapped /t/’s in similar positions:

citation



A word ending with a _**t**_ may be pronounced as a llap if the following word begins


with a vowel. In rapid or informal speech, the italicized _**is**_ of the following sentences


are usually pronounced as flaps: I


I go _**t**_ a charge oitf of that.


The lighf at the end of the tunnel.

**4. Deletion**


In the phonological process of **deletion,** units which occur in some contexts are lost


in others. In many cases, deletion processes change the syllable structure of a word,


thereby creating preferred types of syllable patterns.


The following are some of the most typical environments for deletion:


   - Loss of /t/ when /nt/ is between two vowels or before a syllabic [1,];


_**N**_ winter, Toronto, enter, mantle


   - Loss of _**Ixl**_ or /d/ when they occur second in a sequence or cluster of three


consonants:


_**It/**_ restless, listless, exactly


_**Id/**_ windmill, kindness, hands


   - Deletion of word-final _**It/**_ or _**Id/**_ in clusters of two at a word boundary when the


following word begins with a consonant:


**Deletion**


East side


blind man


wild boar


However, when the following word begins with a vowel, there is no deletion. Instead,


resyllabification occurs.


**No deletion (resyllabitication)**


Eas/twend


blin/dweye


wil/d^ass


**238** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-238-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


In some cases different phonetic forms of the same morpheme in English are derived


by segment deletion rules, as the following examples illustrate:


A B


sign[sajn] signature [signacar]


design [dazajn] designation [dezignsan]


paradigm [phasradajm] paradigmatic [ph£eradigmaef9k]


In none of the words in column A is there a phonetic [g], but in each corresponding


word in column B a [g] occurs. Our knowledge of English phonology accounts for these


phonetic differences. The “[g]-no [g]” alternation is regular, and we apply it to words


that we never have heard. Suppose someone says:


“He was a salignant [salignant] man.”


Not knowing what the word means you might ask:


“Why, did he salign [salajn] somebody?”


It is highly doubtful that a speaker of English would pronounce the verb form without


the _**-ant**_ as [salign], because the phonological rules of English would delete the /g/ when


it occurred in this context. This rule might be stated as:


Delete a /g/ when it occurs before a final nasal consonant.


The rule is even more general, as evidenced by the pair _**gnostic**_ [nastik] and _**agnostic**_


[aegnastik].


This more general rule may be stated as:


Delete a /g/ when it occurs word initially before a nasal consonant or before a


word-final nasal.


Chapter 5 English Phonology • **239**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-239-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


Given this rule, the phonemic representation of the stems in _**sign/signature,**_


_**design/designation, resigii/resignation, repugn/repugnant, phlegm/phlegmatic, paradigm/**_


_**paradigmatic, diaphragm/diaphragmatic,**_ and _**gnosis/agnostic**_ will include a phonemic


/g/ that will be deleted by the regular rule if a prefix or suffix is not added. By stating


the class of sounds that follow the /g/ (nasal consonants) rather than any specific nasal


consonant, the rule deletes the /g/ before both /m/ and /n/.


The phonological rules that delete whole segments, add segments and features, and


change features account for the various phonetic forms of some morphemes. The


following words further illustrate this point:


A **B**


bomb /bamb/ [bam] bombardier /bambodir/ [bambodir]


iamb /ajasmb/ [ajaem] iambic /ajaembik/ [ajccmbok]


crumb /krAmb/ [khrAin] crumble /krAmbl/ [khAmbol]


A speaker of English knows when to pronounce a /IV and when not to. The relationship


between the pronunciation of the A words and their B counterparts is regular and can


be accounted for by the following rule:


Delete a word-final /b/ when it occurs after an /m/.


Notice that the underlying phonemic representation of the A and B stems is the same


and contains the /b/. The rules that delete the segments are general phonological rules,


but their application to phonemic representations results in different phonetic forms of


the same morpheme.


**5. Epenthesis**


Epenthesis is the insertion of a vowel or consonant segment within an existing string


of segments. Although less frequent than deletion in English, epenthesis is by no means


uncommon. The most important type of epenthesis in English occurs in certain


morphophonologieal sequences such as the regular plural and past tense endings. Here

an epenthetic schwa _**h/**_ is added to break up clusters of sibilants or alveolar stops.


Progressive assimilation alone will not make the morphological endings sufficiently


salient. Thus for the plural endings, for which we can posit an underlying /Z/ morpheme,


we have:



Assimilation

And for regular past tense, for which


we have:


Assimilation


look -t- {D} = /lokt/

can posit an underlying {D} morpheme,

hand + {D} = /haendod/



Two rather peculiar sound changes take place in the pronunciation of certain sounds


in context. The first is a reversal of the order of sounds, for example, [lesk] into [asks]


for _**ask.**_ This reversal of sounds is known as metathesis. The second is the deletion of


duplicate elements or syllables, a form of dissimilation known as haplology. These sound


changes are uncommon. A few of the more common ones are listed below. They are


not commonly found in the speech of cultivated speakers.

relevant    - [rcvolonl]


irrelevant     - [irevolont]

perspire    - [prospaia-]


perspiration     - [prespa-rei/n]


pronounce    - [pamauns]

**7. Aspiration**




[misipi]


[mis smie]


[prabli]


[pa^tikysli]


[simoli]



One example of how environment can affect the articulation of a sound involves the


stop consonants /p, t, k/. Thus far, we have distinguished these sounds from /b, d, g/


in terms of voicing. However, an additional significant feature of voiceless stop


consonants in English is aspiration, the brief puff of air that accompanies the allophones


of /p, t, k/ in words such as _**pan, tan,**_ and _**key.**_ The presence or absence of aspiration


is easier to demonstrate with the bilabial stops /p/ and /b/. If an English speaker says


_**pie,**_ the aspiration will often extinguish a lighted match or move a strip of paper placed


in front of the speaker s mouth. If the same speaker says _**buy**_, it will not noticeably affect


the flame or the paper. Since /t/ and /k/ are articulated farther back in the mouth than


/p/, the aspiration is harder to demonstrate visually than for /p/, but it is nonetheless


present and salient.


In general, then, we can say that the voiced stop consonants are not aspirated whereas


the voiceless stop consonants are. However, we need to further qualify this statement,


since the occurrence of aspiration with /p, l, k/ depends on the position of the consonant


within a word. Try saying the following words, in which /p, t, k/ occur word initially


(column 1) and at the beginning of a stressed syllable(column 2):

2

Chapter 5 English Phonology • **243**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-243-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-243-0.png)


Approximately 40 common function words vary in pronunciation according to whether


they are stressed and strong or unstressed and weak. For example, consider the


pronunciations of the operator, or auxiliary verb, _**cun**_ in these sentences:


(1) a. I 'can 'do it. 'Who 'says I 'can’t,


b. 'Can I? In'deed I 'can.


(2) a. 'Who 'can?


b. We 'all 'can.


(3) a. '1 can 'do it.


b. 'What can we 'do?


In the sentences of group 1 _**can**_ is accented. The word is deliberately accented for


emphasis or insistence. In group 2 _**can**_ is stressed but not accented. The emphasis is on


the subject - _**who, we all**_ - but _**can**_ must be stressed in final position. In the sentences


of group 3 _**can**_ is typically unstressed; it occurs between stressed words.


When _**can**_ is stressed, whether accented or not, it is pronounced /keen/. We call this


the **full form** of the word. When unstressed, it has a reduced **form** which we represent


as /kon/.


This function word, then, is accented when the speaker chooses to emphasize it, as


in (la, b), above. It must be stressed, even though not accented, in final position, as in


(2a, b). It is mostly unstressed (and reduced) when it is adjacent to a stressed word, but


in initial position or in a deliberate manner of speaking it may be stressed.

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-244-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-244-0.png)


**1. Definition of intonation**


Intonation is often called the melody of language since it refers to the pattern of _**pitch**_


changes that we use when we speak. If you listen to someone speaking, you will notice


that there are many changes in pitch. These pitch changes are called _**intonation patterns**_


and play an important role in conveying meaning. Languages that use pitch to signal a


difference in meaning between words are referred to as tone languages. English does not


use pitch in this way. Nevertheless pitch changes do contribute significantly to the


meaning of English sentences. These changes in pitch in English occur over entire clauses


or sentences and different pitch patterns can signal very different meanings for the same


sentence.


In the following sections, we introduce some of the basic intonation patterns of


English. We represent these patterns with arrows.


**2. Final intonation**


_**Rising-falling intonation**_ Listen to yourself when you say the following sentence:


Susan bought a new sweater.


Notice that the pitch of your voice rises at the major sentence stress, the first syllable


of the word ‘sweater’, and falls over the second syllable of this word. The pitch of the


entire sentence is referred to as the _**intonation pattern.**_ The pattern in this sentence is


_**rising-falling.**_ It is the most common intonation pattern in English and is characteristic


of simple declarative sentences, commands and questions that begin with a _**wh-**_ word, such


as ‘who’, ‘what’, ‘when’, ‘where’, ‘why’, or ‘how’. Say the sentences below, concentrating


on the pitch change at the word receiving major sentence stress.


He wants to go home.


She gave him live dollars for it.


Chapter 5 English Phonology* **245**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-245-full.png)

**ialHjjlbl**


What English Language Teachers Should Know


Give her a sweater.


**What do you want to do with it?**


In each case, the pitch rises at the major sentence stress and falls over the remaining


part of the sentence. This descent in pitch can be rather abrupt, especially when it must


be accomplished over just one syllable as is the case with ‘home’ in the first sentence.


When the voice falls to the bottom of the pitch range, it usually indicates that the speaker


has finished speaking.


_**Rising intonation**_ Listen to the pitch of your voice when you say the following


sentence:


Did Susan buy a new sweater?


Notice again that the pitch of your voice rises at the major sentence stress. However,


rather than a sharp decline in pitch level after the stressed syllable, as with the


rising-falling intonation pattern, the voice continues to rise. The intonation pattern in this


case is _**rising**_ and is characteristic of questions that require a simple yes or no answer.


In order to familiarize yourself with this pattern, say the following sentences,


concentrating on the pitch of your voice. We represent this intonation pattern with a rising


arrow.


Does lie want to go home?


— — -------------^ o
Did she give him five dollars for it?


           - ------- -- ----- ©

Couldn’t she have seen him?


Do you want to give it to him?


Do you think he wants it?


**246**  - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-246-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.k](http://www.amoriteaching.co.k)


This intonation contour is used to express doubt. That is, the speaker is not sure what


the answer to the question is and would like the information supplied. We can turn a


simple statement into a yes/no question through the use of rising intonation. For example,


if we say:


John left town.


with a rising intonation, we are not making a statement. We are expressing some doubt


regarding the truth of the statement, indicating to the listener that a response is required.


Thus, we can see that intonation patterns can contribute to sentence meaning in English.


**3. Non-final intonation**


_**Rising-falling intonation**_


Complex sentences often have two separate intonation patterns. An example of such


a sentence is provided below.


Because of his athletic ability, he was given a scholarship.


Here the pitch rises and falls on the word ‘ability’, and also on the word ‘scholarship’.


On ‘scholarship’, the pitch drifts to the bottom of the pitch range, while on ‘ability’, the


pitch does not fall nearly as far. The intonation contour on the first half of the sentence


is a _**non-final rising-falling contour.**_ The following sentences usually have two intonation


contours - the non-final contour on the first phrase and the final contour on the second.


We represent these contours slightly differently, as shown by the arrows. The arrow on


the non-final contour does not go as far down as it does for the final contour. (We indicate


that there should be two separate contours by the use of //.)


When John left the house // it was raining.


'l lie man you say you met yesterday // lias left town.


/\ner we nave dinner // we n go to a movie.


Chapter 5 English Phonology • **247**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-247-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


Thus, a fall at the end of the sentence to the lowest pitch possible indicates that our


thought is complete, and a fall that is not to the bottom of the pitch range indicates that


we still have more to say. It is very useful to bear this in mind when listening to your


students’ pronunciation. Our experience is that if a student does not have a large enough


drop in pitch in ending a sentence, native speakers will expect that there is more to come.


This can lead to embarrassing silences and communication breakdowns.


_**Continuation rise**_ Say the following sentence.


Susan bought a new sweater, new shoes, and a new dress.


This intonation contour of this sentence is termed a _**continuation rise**_ and is often


used with lists. The pitch of the voice rises slightly on each noun of the list, indicating


that we are not yet finished speaking. On the final noun of the list, we find the familiar


rise-fall. Pronounce the following sentences in order to familiarize yourself with this


pattern.

He bought apples, peaches, pears, and oranges.


I'll have two pencils, a black pen, and some ink.


We went to Paris, Brussels, Amsterdam, and London.


I saw Esther, Jane, Neil, and Susan.


Some complex sentences display a continuation rise on the first half rather than a


rise-fall.


11 you want to get ahead, you have to work hard.


In this sentence, the intonation may rise slightly on the word ‘ahead’. Following a


slight pause, we find the rising-falling contour on the next clause.


**248** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-248-full.png)

www.amoriteaching.co.kr~>'


**4. Tag questions**


Tag questions can display either final rising-falling or final rising intonation contours.


Their meaning will differ depending on which of these contours is used. Pronounce the


following tag questions with the intonation contours indicated:


Deanna’s helpful, isn’t she?


Deanna’s helpful, isn’t she?


The first sentence, with a rising contour, indicates that the speaker genuinely does


not know whether Deanna is helpful and wants the listener to provide this information.


The second sentence, with a rising-falling contour, indicates that the speaker believes that


Deanna is helpful and is merely eliciting confirmation from the listener.


Tag questions with rising-falling intonation are very often used to begin conversations.


For example, in attempting to begin a conversation with a stranger, one might say:


Lovely day, isn’t it?


In uttering this sentence, the speaker is not demanding an answer to a question but


rather opening the lines of communication.

**01 Allophones of [I] in English**


There are three distinct allophonic forms of [l] in many English dialects, shown here as

[l], [l], and [+]. Figure out the rule that determines when each allophone of the phoneme

/!/ occurs. To do so, look at the sounds that occur on either side of the [l]s, and write


what determines when each sound occurs.


[lip] lip


[lay] lie


[plei] play


[klu] clue

[pit] pm


[ayi] isle


[blu] blue


[glu] glue


Now look up each of the symbols and write the description of each of these allophones

(in terms of place and manner of articulation and voicing).


[I] occurs after a voiceless consonant. It is a called a voiceless alveolar liquid. (It sometimes occurs


as the voiceless alveolar fricative, indicated by the synibolf • ].)


[I] occurs at the end of a word/syllable or after a vowel. It is called an alveolar lateral fricative. (IPA


calls it a velarized alveolar lateral approximat'd.)


[1] occurs at the beginning of words/syllables and after voiced consonants. So [1] is likely the


defaull/basic sound, the phoneme, which becomes either [1] (voiceless, after a voiceless sound, by


assimilation) or [i] (after a vowel, or at the end of a word).


Students can try to determine which rule is more likely for the occurrence of ft], either after a vowel


or at the end of a word, and why.

**02 Clear I and Dark 1**


Many speakers of English have two variants of [1]. One, called _**clear l,**_ is transcribed as

[1] in the following data. The other, called _**dark l,**_ is transcribed with [+]. Examine the

data, and answer the questions that follow.



a. [L\jf] ‘life’


b. [lip] ‘leap’


c. [lu:z] ‘lose’


d. [ilowp] ‘elope’


e. [dilAjt] ‘delight’


f. [slip] ‘sleep’



g. [p'li] ‘pill’


h. [fii] ‘feel’


i. [help] ‘help’


j. [bA+k| ‘bulk’


k. [sowid] ‘sold’


l. [fei] ‘full’



Do [I] and [i] belong to separate phonemes or are they allophones of the same phoneme?


If you think they belong to separate phonemes, answer question (A). If you think they

are allophones of the same phoneme, answer question (B).


A. List the evidence that makes your case for considering [1] and |T] as separate phonemes.


B. State the distribution of [I] and [+] in words.


C. Which variant makes the best underlying form? Why?


A. There is no compelling evidence for this position, since the two varieties of /I arc phonetically similar


and in complementary distribution.


B. The ‘clear’ variety [1] is found in syllable onsets. The ‘dark’ variety [i] is found in codas.


C. There is no strong reason to choose one variety over the other as basic. One approach is to consider


the clear [1] as basic. The ‘dark’ feature is added by rule to any lateral found in coda position.

/priektas/ /insist/ /aton/


B. The final syllable of the verb is stressed if it ends with a consonant cluster; otherwise, the stress


falls on the penultimate syllable.


C. All of the final vowels in column C are tense (long or diphthong) vowels. Thus, the analysis in


(B) must be modified to read: Stress the final syllable of a verb if its vowel is tense (long or


diphthong) or followed by a consonant cluster; otherwise stress the penultimate syllable.

### **The History of English**


/
CHECKPOINT


**Great Vowel Shift**


A major change in English that resulted in new phonemic representations of words


and morphemes took place approximately between 1400 and 1600. It is known as the


_**Great Vowel Shift.**_ The seven long, or tense, vowels of Middle English underwent the


following change:


Shift Example


Middle Modern Middle Modern


English English English English


[i:] -> [aj] [mi:s] —- [majs] mice


[u:] -> [aw] [mu:s] —►[maws] mouse


[e:]      - [i:] [ge:s] -> [gi:s] geese


[o:] -> [u:] [go:s] — [gu:s] goose


[£:]     - [e:] [breiken] —►[bre:k] break


[o:]     - [o:] [bn: ken] —1►[bro:k] broke


[a:]      - [e:] [na:mo] —> [ne:m] name


By diagramming the Great Vowel Shift on a vowel chart, we can see that the high


vowels [i:] and [u:] became the diphthongs [aj] and [aw], while the long vowels underwent


an increase in tongue height, as if to fill in the space vacated by the high vowels. In


addition, [a:] was fronted to become [e:].

**Semantic Change**
_**J**_


**1. Generalization or Semantic extensions**


Extensions in meaning occur when the set of appropriate contexts or referents for


a word increases. These are frequently the result of generalizing from the specific case


to the class of which the specific case is a member. An example of this type would


be the change in meaning undergone by the Old English (OE) word _**docga,**_ modern day


_**dog.**_ In OE _**docga**_ referred to a particular breed of dog, while in modern usage it refers


to the class of dogs as a whole. Thus the set of contexts in which the word may be


used has been _**extended**_ from the specific case (a particular breed of dog) to the general


class (all dogs, dogs in general). A similar type of change has affected modern English


_**bird.**_ Though it once referred to a particular species of bird, it now is used for the general


class.


**2. Specialization or Semantic reductions**


Reductions occur when the set of appropriate contexts or referents for a word


decreases. Historically specking, this is relatively less common than extensions of


meaning, though it still occurs fairly frequently. An example of a semantic reduction


would be the Old English word _**hand,**_ modern day _**hound.**_ While this word originally


**258** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-258-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


referred to dogs in general, its meaning has now been restricted, for the most part, to


a few particular breeds of dog. Thus its usage has become less general over time.


Similarly, the word _**worm**_ once was used for any crawling creature but is now restricted


to a particular type of crawling creature.


Additional examples of this type of change include the Modem English words _**skyline**_


and _**girl. Sky’line**_ originally referred to the horizon in general. It has since been restricted


to particular types of horizons - ones in which the outlines of hills, buildings, or other


structures appear. In Middle English the word corresponding to modern-day _**girl**_ referred


to young people of either sex. A semantic reduction has resulted in its current, less


general, meaning.


**3. Amelioration or Semantic elevations**


Semantic elevations occur when a word takes on somewhat grander connotations over


time. For example, the word _**knight**_ originally meant ‘youth’ or ‘military follower’ 

relatively powerless and unimportant people. The meaning of _**knight**_ has since been


elevated to refer to people of a somewhat more romantic and impressive status. Similarly,


the word _**chivalrous**_ was at one time synonymous with _**warlike;**_ it now refers to more


refined properties such as fairness, generosity, and honor. A particularly good example


of this type is the shift in meaning undergone by the word _**squire.**_ The Middle English


(ME) equivalent of _**squire**_ was used to refer to a knight’s attendant, the person who held


his shield and armor for him. In Modern English, however, a squire is a country gentleman


or large landowner. Thus the meaning of _**squire**_ has changed rather drastically over time,


acquiring a socially more positive meaning.


_U._ **Pejoration or Semantic degradations**


Semantic degradations are the opposite of semantic elevations; they occur when a word


acquires a more pejorative meaning over time. Examples of words whose meanings have


been degraded include _**lust, wench**_ and _**silly.**_ In OE _**lust**_ simply meant ‘pleasure’, making


its current association with sinfulness a degradation of the original meaning. Similarly,


the ME word _**wenche(l)**_ meant ‘female child’ and later ‘female servant’. It then came to


Chapter 6 The History of English • **259**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-259-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


mean ‘lewd female’ or ‘woman of a low social class’. The word _**silly**_ is a particularly


interesting example of semantic degradation because the social force of the word has


almost completely reversed. Whereas in ME _**silly**_ meant something akin to ‘happy, blessed,


innocent’, it now is more on a par with ‘foolish, inane, absurd’. Thus the connotations


of _**silly**_ have gone from strongly positive to strongly negative in a matter of a few


centuries.

**01** **Great Vowel Shift**


Although a good many changes of various kinds affected the English vowel system as


it developed from Middle English to Modem English, those changes to which the long


vowels were subject are especially worthy of note. The seven Middle English long vowels

underwent a remarkably systematic shift in their place of articulation, a shift for which

there was no cause that we can discover.


Show the changes effected by the Great Vowel Shift by writing the appropriate phonetic


symbols in the brackets:

[ ]>[ ] as in _**goose:**_ ME [go:s]>Mod E [

[ ]>[ ] as in _**break**_ : ME [broken] >Mod E [


[ ]>[ ] as in _**broke:**_ ME [bro:ken] >Mod E [


[ ]>[ j as in _**name:**_ ME [na:ma] >Mod E [


[i:]>[ai] as in _mice:_ ME [mi:s]>Mod E [mais]


[u:J>[au] as in _mouse:_ ME [mu:s]>Mod E [maus]


[e:J > [i:J as in _geese:_ ME [ge:s]>Mod E [gi:s]


**[o:J >[u:] as** in _goose:_ **ME [go:s]>Mod E [gu:s]**

**02** **Trisyllabic Shortening and Great Vowel Shift**


Trisyllabic laxing or Trisyllabic shortening may refer to the process in English whereby

tense vowels (which are long vowels or diphthongs) become lax (i.c. short monophthongs

(lax) in word formation) when followed by two syllables, of which the first syllable is

unstressed. Later in Middle English this process was expanded, and applied to all vowels

when two or more syllables followed. This Middle English sound change occurred before

the Great Vowel Shift (GVS), the massive sound change that affected the pronunciation


of long vowels. The shift can be roughly summarized as follows: [a:] —*■ [as:] —► [e:] —>


[e:] — [i:] —> [ay] and [o:] —► [o:] — [u:] —* [aw]. As a result of these changes, the

pairs of vowels related by trisyllabic laxing often bear little resemblance to each other

in Modern English; however, originally they always bore a consistent relationship.


Compare the underlined vowels of pairs of words in (l)-(3) and discuss why they are

pronounced differently.


( 1 ) divine - divinity, line - linear


( 2 ) serene - serenity, supreme - supremacy


(3) sane - sanity, explain - explanatory


The underlined vowel used to be /i:/ in (1), _lc:l_ in (2), [ax] in (3), but it became short or lax when


followed by two syllables via Trisyllabic shortening, as in the second element of each pair. But the


long vowels underwent the GVS in the first element, resulting in /ay/, /i:/, and _Id_ (or /ey/), respectively.


**262** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-262-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know

##### **Glossary of English** **Linguistics Terminology**


- _**accidental gaps**_


Non-occurring but possible forms of a language, (e.g., in English, _block)._


_**• accomplishment verb**_


A type of dynamic verb, expressing action that has a natural end point, (e.g., _build_ **,**


_paint)._


- _**achievement verb**_


A type of dynamic verb, expressing action that occurs instantaneously, (e.g., _bounce_ **,**


_kick)_ or involves a preliminary activity culminating in the action denoted by the verb


(e.g., _find, cross)_


_**• accusative case**_


This is the form that pronouns occur in when they function as Direct Object or as


the Complement of a preposition, (e.g., _I called him.) •_


- _**acronyms**_


Acronyms are formed by taking the initial letters of (some or all) the words in a phrase


or title and pronouncing them as a word, (e.g., UNICEF for United Nations


International Children’s Emergency Fund, Cl DA for Canadian International


Development Agency, NATO for North Atlantic Treaty organization, and AIDS for


acquired immune deficiency syndrome).


- _**activity verb**_


A type of dynamic verb, expressing action that can go on for an indefinite period of


time. The action may be one of continuity (e.g., _run_ **,** _walk)_ or inherent change _(e.g.,_


_decline, grow)_


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **263**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-263-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**adjacency requirement**_


For the Case assignor to assign Case to NPs, the two must be adjacent.


 - _**adjunct**_


In X-bar syntax, a modifier that occurs between any specifier and complement within


a phrase (e.g., the adjective _tall_ in the NP _the tall math student._ The term adjunct


designates a position (like subject, direct object, etc.), not a class of words (like


determiner, adjective, etc.).


 - _**“ffix**_


An appendix added to the beginning or end of a word, (e.g., _-ness_ is an affix, more


specifically a suffix, which can be added to the adjective _happy,_ deriving a noun,


namely _happiness._ And _dis-_ is an affix, more specifically a prefix, which can be added


to the verb _like,_ deriving another verb, namely _dislike._


 - _**affricate**_


A consonant that is articulated via a complete oral closure followed by a slow release


with a friction noise, as in the first and the last sounds of _church._


 - _**agent**_


A thematic role which indicates the ‘doer’ or instigator of an action denoted by a


predicate in a proposition.


 - _**allomorph**_


Any of the forms that are the members of the same morpheme (with the same meaning).


The forms [s], [z], and [oz] arc the allomorphs of the plural morpheme (e.g. _cats_ [kaets],


_dogs_ [dogz], and _bushes_ [bojoz]). •


 - _**allophone**_


Any of the phonetically similar sounds that are the relization of a single phoneme in


varying contexts. For example, [ph] and [p] are the allophones of the phoneme /p/;


the former occurs at the beginning of a stressed syllable (e.g. _pay_ [phe]) and the other


elsewhere (e.g. _sport_ [sport]).


**264** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-264-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.](http://www.amoriteaching.co)


- _**alveolar**_


A consonant articulated in the region behind the upper front teeth (e.g. /t, s, n/).


- _**ambisyllabic**_


A consonant belonging to two syllables, (e.g., /n/ in _phoneme_ [fonim].)


- _**amelioration**_


Semantic change in which the connotation of a word becomes elevated (e.g., the word


_knight_ originally meant ‘youth’ or ‘military follower’. The meaning of _knight_ has since


been elevated to refer to people of a somewhat more romantic and impressive status).


- _**analytic sentence**_


One that is necessarily true as a result of the words in it (e.g., _A triangle has three_


_sides)._


_**• anaphora**_


Describing a linguistic expression (the anaphor) that must have another expression to


refer to (the antecedent) (e.g., all reflexive pronouns are anaphors).


- _**antecedent**_


A linguistic expression that another expression refers to (e.g., _They_ is the antecedent


of _each other_ in _They ran into each other). •_


 - _**antepenult**_


The third syllable from the end of the word (e.g., an.te.pe.nult)


° _**anterior**_


A pice feature that characterizes sounds articulated in front of alveopalatal region, (e.g.,


labial, dental, and alveolar sounds are [+ant]).


 - _**antonymy**_


Two words whose senses differ only in the value of a single semantic feature (e.g.,


_man_ and _boy_ differ only in [±adult]).


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **265**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-265-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**approximant**_


An articulation in which one articulator is close to another but not so close as to create


a turbulent airstream (friction). In English, liquids, /l, r/, and glides, /j, w/, are


approximants.


 - _**argument**_


A participant (role player) in a proposition.


 - _**aspect**_


A grammatical notion which signifies the way in which a situation expressed by a verb,


or a verb and its Complement(s), in a particular sentence or clause is viewed, for


example as an ongoing process (progressive aspect), or as a situation located in the


past, but relevant to the present (present perfect aspect). In English aspect is


syntactically expressed especially by the aspectual auxiliaries _**be**_ and _**have,**_ combined


with a verb ending in _**-ing**_ or _**-ed.**_


 - _**aspiration**_


A puff of air following the release of a voiceless stop at the beginning of a stressed


syllable (e.g., _**pet**_ [phet]


 - _**assimilation**_


Any phonological process by which one segment becomes more like a neighboring


segment (e.g., Vowel Nasalization).


 - _**atelic verb**_


A verb expressing something with no natural end point. Stative verbs and activty verbs


are atelic. •


 - _**back-formation**_


A process that creates a new word by removing a real or supposed affix from another


word in the language, (e.g., _**editor, peddler**_ and _**swindler).**_


**266** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-266-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


- _**benefactive**_


An NP representing an entity that benefits from an action (e.g., _Mary_ in _John baked_


_a cake for Mary)._


_**• bilabial**_


A sound produced with both lips (e.g., /p, b, m/).


- _**binary/complementary antonyms**_


Antonyms that describe all possibilities along a single dimension (e.g., _sane_ and


_insane)._


- _**binding principles**_


Principle A: The principle that constrains the interpretation of reflexive pronouns and


is formulated as: A reflexive pronoun must have an antecedent that c-commands it in


the same minimal S.


Principle B: The principle that constrains the interpretation of pronominals and is


formulated as: A pronominal must not have an antecedent that c-commands it in the


same minimal S.


° _**blend**_


A word that is created from parts of two already existing items (e.g., _brunch_ from


_breakfast_ and _lunch) •_


_**• case filter**_


Every overt NP must be assigned Case by such appropriate Case assignors as a


preposition, the finite tense, complementizer _for_, and a transitive verb.


° _**c-command**_


A node c-commands its sisters and their descendants (X is a _descendant_ of Y if X


is dominated by Y)


 - _**cleft sentence**_


A sentence which conforms to the following pattern:


_It_ + form of _be_ + focus _who/that_


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **267**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-267-full.png)

sssoi What English Language Teachers Should Know


(e.g., _It was in Brazil that I was so happy_ is a cleft version of _1 was so happy in_


_Brazil)._


     - _**clipping**_


A process that shortens a polysyllabic word by deleting one or more syllables, (e.g.,


_doc, ad, auto, lab, sub, deli, porn, demo,_ and _condo)._


     - _**closed syllable**_


A syllable that ends in one or more consonant sounds (i.e. a syllable with a coda (e.g.,


the first syllable of _Atlanta_, and the last syllable of _geminate))._


_**• coda**_


Whatever comes after the nucleus of the syllable (e.g., /t/ in _cat)._


     - _**cooperative principle**_


The assumption that participants in a conversation are cooperating with each other.


     - _**complement**_


In X-bar syntax, a modifier that always occurs next to the head of the phrase, whether


it precedes or follows the head (e.g., the noun _math_ in _the tall math student)._ The


term _complement_ designates a position (like subject, direct object, etc.), not a class of


words (like determiner, adjective, etc.).


     - _**complementary distribution**_


The relation between two items that never occur in the same environment (e.g., in


English [th] always occurs before a stressed vowel, but [r] never does). •


     - _**complementiser**_


A word that introduces a clause that functions as a Complement to a verb, (e.g., _that_


is a complementiser in the following sentence: / _believe that the sun will shine._ The


string _that the sun will shine_ functions as Direct Object). There are four


complementisers in English, which together form a subclass of the set of subordinating


conjunctions: _that, if whether_ and _for._


**268**   - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-268-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


_**consonant cluster reduction**_


Deletion of a stop consonant from a series of consonants (e.g., _hand me_ [ha:nd mi]


—* [hsen mi]).


_**constancy under negation**_


A test for presupposition: Sentence A presupposes sentence B if both “A” and “not


A” assume the truth of B (e.g., _Ralph regrets buying an SUV_ and _Ralph doesn’t regret_


_buying an SUV_ both assume the truth of _Ralph bought an SUJ/)._


_**constituent**_


A string of words which syntactically behaves as a unit.


_**constituency test**_


A test by means of which a string of words can be shown to behave as a unit.


_**constituent response test**_


A constituency test which stipulates that only constituents can be used as responses


to open interrogatives.


_**content word**_


A word that contributes to the lexical meaning of an utterance; usually stressed (lexical


morphemes such as nouns, verbs, adjectives, adverbs).


_**contrastive**_


Of a relation between two segments that can occur in the same environment to produce


different meanings. From _pig_ and _big_, we conclude that their initial sounds are


contrastive.


_**control**_


In non-finite clause with a PRO subject which has an antecedent, the antecedent is


said to be the controller of PRO, and conversely PRO is to be controlled by its


antecedent (e.g., in a structure like ‘John decided PRO to quit’, _John_ is the controller


of PRO, and conversely PRO is controlled by _John)._


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **269**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-269-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**conversational implicature**_


Information that is conveyed through inference but is not actually said.


 - _**conversational maxims**_


Four principles (Quality, Quantity, Relation, and Manner) in terms of which speakers


interpret utterances.


 - _**converse antonyms/relational opposites**_


Antonyms that describe the relationship between two entities from opposite perspectives


(e.g., _employer_ and _employee)._


_**• conversion/category extension/functional shift/ zero-derivation**_


A word-formation process whereby a word in one category is extended to a new


category, at which point it can also be inflected like other members of the new category


(e.g., _table_ (N) —> _table_ (V) enables _The committee tabled the discussion)._


 - _**coreference**_


The condition of two expressions having the same referent (e.g., _President o f the U.S._


and _Commander-in-Chief). •_


 - _**daughter**_


A relation between two nodes in a tree diagram. Node B is the daughter of A if A


directly dominates B: e.g.,


A


C


 - _**deixis**_


The ability of one expression to refer systematically to two or more distinct entities


in the same context (e.g., _you_ and /), depending on the speaker’s point of view.


° _**derivational morpheme**_


All prefixes and all noninflectional suffixes; derivational suffixes typically change the


category of the root (e.g., _-ize: critic_ is a noun, but _criticize_ is a verb).


**270** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-270-full.png)

dp i
www.amoriteaching.co.kr^


- _**diphthong**_


Two adjacent vowels that form the nucleus of a single syllable. The diphthong


phonemes in English are /ai, ao, oi/ as in _buy, cow,_ and _boy._


_**• direct illocutionary act**_


One performed by an utterance whose syntactic form matches its illocutionary force


(e.g., an imperative used to issue a directive). Any other kind is an indirect illocutionary


act.


- _**dissimilation**_


The phonological process that occurs when adjacent sounds become more different from


each other (rather than more similar, as is the case with assimilation).


- _**distinctive features**_


Two-valued dimensions that serve as basic building blocks of segments (e.g., _**/ml**_ and


/n/ are both [+nasal] and [+voice]).


- _**dominate**_


A relation between two nodes in a tree diagram. Node A dominates B if you can trace


a line up from B to A without descending: e.g.,


**A**


**C** **D**


**F** **B**


- _**do so-substitution**_


A syntactic process whereby a string of words is replaced by _do so._ (e.g., in _Maria_


_bought a present fo r her daughter, and Sara did so too_ the pro form _do so_ replaces


the words _bought a present fo r her daughter). *_


- _**dynamic verb**_


A verb that expresses action rather than a state. A dynamic verb may be an activity


verb, achievement verb, or accomplishment verb.


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **271**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-271-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**elision**_


A process in which a consonant is left out in order to make the articulation easier


(e.g., _**fifth**_ is pronounced [fie] instead of [fife]).


 - _**ellipsis**_


Use of a structure with an element that is missing but can be recovered from a previous


part of the discourse (e.g., _John ate but I didn’t feat])._


_**• embedding**_


A Clause found within a higher clause (e.g., _1 told him [what he should do])._


 - _**end-focus principle**_


The new or most important idea or message in a piece of information should be placed


towards the end, where in speech the nucleus of the tone unit normally falls.


 - _**endocentric compound**_


A compound whose rightmost component identifies the general class to which the


meaning of the entire word belongs, (e.g., _dump truck_ is a type of truck).


 - _**end-weight principle**_


The more weighty part(s) of a sentence should be placed towards the end. Otherwise


the sentence may sound awkward and unbalanced. The ‘weight’ of an element can be


defined in terms of its length (i.e. the number of its syllables or words).


 - _**entailment**_


A proposition expressed in a sentence that follows _necessarily_ from another sentence


(e.g., _John murdered Bill_ entails _Bill died)._


_**• epenthesis**_


The insertion of a segment into a series. Vowel epenthesis inserts a vowel between


two consonants, and consonant epenthesis inserts a consonant between two vowels. •


 - _**ergative verb**_


Such verbs, which allow the object of a transitive clause to be a subject of an


**272** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-272-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


intransitive clause without changing voice, are called ergative, or change-of-state, verbs.


Ergative verbs take either agents or undergoers of the action as subjects, (e.g., Her


high C _shattered_ the glass. The glass _shattered.)_


- _**existential there**_


The word _there_ that we find in propositions that are concerned with the existence of


people, things, etc. (e.g., _There is a cat in the garage_ **).**


- _**experiencer**_


A thematic role which indicates the entity that experiences the action of event denoted


by a predicate in a proposition.


° _**explicit performative**_


An utterance containing a performative verb used in its performative sense (e.g., /


_apologize for...)._ Any other utterance is a non-explicit performative.


- _**exocentric compound**_


A compound whose meaning does not follow from the meaning of its parts, (e.g.,


_redneck_, since its reference is not a type of neck).


- _**expressed locutionary act**_


One actually expressing the propositional content for the illocutionary act involved


(e.g., a promise must predicate a future act of the speaker, so / _promise I ’ll help you_


_with your homework_ is expressed). Any other locutionary act is implied (e.g., _I promise_


_you won_ **V** _have to do your homework alone)._


- _**extraposition**_


A transformation that moves a relative clause or prepositional phrase away form the


NP it modifies to clause-final position (e.g., _A rumor about you just surfaced_ —> _A_


_rumor just surfaced about you)._


° _**external argument**_


The argument that functions as subject in a proposition.


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **273**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-273-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**/ active verbs**_


A main clause verb that presupposes the truth of the proposition in the subordinate


clause (e.g., / _**realize that you are a coward**_ presupposes ‘You are a coward’).


° _**felicity conditions**_


Conditions that must be met for the valid performance of an illocutionary act (i.e.,


participants and circumstances must be appropriate, participants must have appropriate


intentions, etc.).


6 _**finite**_


A term applied to a verb to indicate that it carries tense.


_•flapping_


_**N**_ and /d/ become [r] when they occur between two vowels, the first of which is


stressed and the second of which is unstressed, (e.g., _**rider**_ [rairor]).


 - _**flouting**_


The intentional violation of a conversational maxim for the purpose of conveying an


unstated proposition (e.g., _**I could eat a horse**_ —> ‘I am hungry’).


_**•foot**_


Each phonological foot starts with a stressed syllable, and continues up to, but not


including, the next stressed syllable.


 - _**free morpheme**_


One that can stand alone as a word (e.g., _**man).**_


_**•free variation**_


Two items that can occur in the same environment without causing a change in meaning


(e.g., [J] and [z] are in free variation in _**garage).**_


_**• fricative**_


A consonant produced with a partial obstruction of the airstream in which the air is


pushed through a narrow constriction, resulting in a friction noise (e.g., /f, s, e/).


**274** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-274-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


_**function word**_


A word that contributes little to the lexical meaning of an utterance; usually unstressed


in a sentence (e.g. grammatical morphemes, such as prepositions, determiners, auxiliary


verbs, and so on).


_**geminate/gemination**_


_**A**_ sequence of two identical segments. In English, it can occur only at morpheme


boundaries (e.g. _**unknown).**_


_**given-new contract**_


_**A**_ discourse principle that states that given or old information in an ongoing discourse


should appear in a sentence before new information.


_**glide**_


_**A**_ segment associated with restricted airflow, but not enough to create turbulence (e.g.,


/y, w/).


_**goal**_


_**A**_ thematic role which indicates the location or entity in the direction of which


something moves.


_**gradable antonyms**_


_**A**_ relationship between opposites where the positive of one term does not necessarily


imply the negative of the other, (e.g. _**rich/poor, fast/slow, young/old, beaut ifhi/ugly).**_


_**grammatical morpheme**_


One whose function is to modify the sense of a lexical morpheme; prepositions, articles,


conjunctions, and affixes are grammatical.


_**head**_


_**A**_ functional label which refers to the principal element in a phrase whose category


determines the category of that phrase.


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **275**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-275-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**heavy NP shift**_


Movement to the right of a noun phrase that is heavy by virtue of containing a


postmodifying prepositional phrase or clause, (e.g., in the following sentence the NP


_**three hibles that were hound in leather**_ has been moved across the PP _**to Rome**_ under


HNPS: _**1 sent _ to Rome [NP three hibles that were bound in leather],**_


 - _**hedges**_


Words or phrases used to indicate that we are not really sure that what we are saying


is sufficiently correct or complete, (e.g., _**as far as**_ / _**know, to cut a long story short,**_


_**I guess).**_


_**• hierarchical structure**_


The grouping of words within phrases (e.g., _**[old [English teacher]]).**_


 - _**homonymy**_


One word-form with two unrelated meanings (e.g., _**key**_ ‘device for disengaging a lock'


and ‘small island').


 - _**hyponymy**_


The sense of one word (the superordinate) being entirely contained in the sense of


another word (the hyponym) (e.g., the sense of _**talk**_ is entirely contained within the


sense of _**mumble).**_


_**° illocutionary act**_


Intended meaning of the utterance that an utterance is understood to have.


 - _**illocutionary force**_


The illocutionary act performed by a given utterance. •


 - _**implicative verb**_


A main clause verb that presupposes a particular attitude on the part of the speaker


toward the proposition in the subordinate clause (e.g., / _**managed to find your keys**_


presupposes ‘finding your keys was difficult,’ vs. _**I happened to find your keys**_


presupposes ‘finding your keys was accidental').


276 - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-276-full.png)

www.amoriteaching.co.kr^^c'


- _**implicature**_


A proposition _implied_ by an utterance but not part of that utterance or a necessary


consequence of that utterance.


- _**indirect illocutionary act**_


Use of a syntactic form that does not match the illocutionary force of an utterance;


especially common when speakers issue directives (e.g., _Could you close the window?_


is indirect; _Close the window_ is indirect).


- _**inflection-node (I-node)**_


The node in a phrase marker that contains features carrying information about


agreement and tense.


- _**inflectional morpheme**_


One of eight suffixes in English that do not change the category of the root and indicate


aspects of the grammatical function of a word (e.g., {PLU}: _chair_ is a noun and plural


_chairs_ is a noun).


- _**instrument**_


An NP representing an entity used to perform an action (e.g., _a knife_ in _John cut the_


_cake with a knife)._


- _**interdentals**_


Sounds made with the tongue placed between the teeth, (e.g., /©/ in _thick,_ _**16/**_ in _though)._


- _**internal argument**_


An a argument that positioned to the right of the verb in English (e.g., a Direct Object


or an Indirect Object). •


- _**intonation**_


A pitch change over an entire sentence; pitch falls in declaratives and _wh-_


interrogalives, but rises in _yes-no_ interrogatives.


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **277**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-277-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**labio-dental**_


An articulation involving the lower lip and the upper front teeth (e.g. /f, v/).


 - _**lexical ambiguity**_


The condition of a word having more than one sense (e.g., _**star**_ means both ‘heavenly


body’ and ‘celebrity’).


 - _**lexical morpheme**_


One having an independent sense; most nouns, verbs, and adjectives are lexical (e.g.,


_**man, run, tall).**_


 - _**literal locutionary act**_


An utterance in which the words mean exactly what they say (i.e., one that is not


sarcastic or exaggerated). Any other locutionary act is nonliteral.


 - _**locutionary act**_


The production of the sounds and words that make up an utterance.


 - _**locative**_


A thematic role that specifies the place where the action or event denoted by a predicate


in a proposition is situated.


 - _**maximal onset principle**_


Where there is a choice, always assign as many consonants as possible to the onset,


and as few as possible to the coda.


 - _**maxim o f manner**_


This maxim states that each participant’s contribution should be expressed in a


reasonably clear fashion; that is, it should not be vague, ambiguous, or excessively


wordy. •


 - _**maxim o f quality**_


This maxim state that each participant’s contribution should be truthful and based on


sufficient evidence.


**278** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-278-full.png)

www.amoriteaching.co.k r 'X vr


_**maxim o f quantity**_


This maxim states that each participant’s contribution to a conversation should be no


more or less informative than required


_**maxim o f relevance**_


This maxim states that each participant’s contribution should be relevant to the subject


of the conversation.


_**metathesis**_


A process that reorders a sequence of segments (e.g., pronouncing _ask_ as [asks].


_**middle verb l**_


A verb that appears to require adverb phrases but also because the syntactic subject


is actually the logical object of the verb.


The bread cuts easily.


*The bread cuts.


Someone cuts the bread.


_**middle verb 2**_


A few stative monotransitive verbs that normally do not allow the passive, (e.g., _have,_


_fit, suit, resemble, equal, mean, contain, hold, comprise, lack)._


_**minimal pair**_


A set of two words that differ in one phoneme (e.g. _chip - tip),_


_**modality**_


A semantic notion which is concerned with such concepts such necessity, possibility,


obligation, permission.


_**morpheme**_


A minimal element of meaning associated with a particular form (e.g., {pel} means


‘push’ as in _repel, compel,_ etc.).


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **279**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-279-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


0 _**nasal**_


A sound produced with a lowered velum so that, when the closure in the mouth is


released, the air rushes out through the nose as well as through the mouth (e.g. the


initial and final sounds of _**moon).**_


_**• nasal assimilation**_


A nasal segment assimilates the place feature of a following consonant (e.g., _**input**_


[inpot] —* [impot]).


e _**negative polarity items**_


An expression that is grammatical in the presence of negation, but ungrammatical in


simple affirmative sentences, (e.g., _**any**_ in _**James does not have any money**_ but _***James**_


_**has any money.).**_


 - _**neutralization**_


Any phonological process that wipes out the contrast between two segments (e.g.,


flapping, vowel reduction).


 - _**nonfinite**_


A term applied to a verb to indicate that it does not carry tense.


**0** _NP_ _**-Movement**_


The displacement of NPs in raising and passive constructions.


 - _**nucleus**_


The most prominent part (peak) of a syllable; most often a vowel or a dipthong.


 - _**obstruent**_


A sound that is articulated with an obstruction in the vocal tract, which is enough to


produce friction noise (e.g. stops, fricatives, and affricates).


 - _**onset**_


The components of a syllable preceding the rhyme (e.g. /bl/ of _**blue).**_


**280** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-280-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.k](http://www.amoriteaching.co.k)


- _**open syllable**_


A syllable with no consonant sound at the end (e.g. _bee)._


- _**palatalization**_


The first sound and second sound in a sequence come together and mutually condition


the creation of a third sound with features from both original sounds.


**Sound A** **+** **Sound B**


**Sound C**


This process occurs most frequently in English when final alveolar consonants such


as /s, z/ and /t, d/ or final alveolar consonant sequences such as /ts, dz/ are followed


by initial palatal /y/. They then become palatalized fricatives and affricates, respectively,


(e.g., _this year, pleasure, procedure, stature)._


- _**palato-alveolar**_


An articulation between the tongue blade and the back of the alveolar ridge (e.g. //,


ctV).


- _**paraphrase**_


A rewording of a sentence that does not alter its meaning (e.g., _Give him the key —*_


_Give the key to him)._


_**• patient**_


An NP representing an entity being acted upon (e.g., _John ate beans)._


- _**pejoration**_


The process in which the meaning of a word becomes less favorable, (e.g., _wench_ used


to mean simply _‘g irl’)._


_**• penult**_


The next-to-last syllable in a word (e.g. _pho.no.lo.g)>)._


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **281**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-281-full.png)

dE gH

soitj- What English Language Teachers Should Know


  - _**performative verb**_


A verb that actually names the illocutionary act it is used to perform (e.g., _state, order,_


_question,_ etc.).


   - _**perloctionary act**_


The effect achieved by an utterance on the hearer.


  - _**phoneme**_


The smallest unit of sound in a language which can distinguish two words, (e.g., in


English, the words _pan_ and _ban_ differ only in their initial sound. Therefore, /p/ and


/b/ are phonemes of English).


  - _**phonological rules**_


Generalizations describing the pronunciation of segments according to their position


(e.g., vowels are lengthened before voiced consonants).


  - _**phonotactic constraints**_


Restrictions on the permissible sequences of segments in a language (e.g., if three


consonants begin a word in English, the first one must be /s/ and the third one must


be a liquid or a glide).


  - _**phrasal verb**_


A complex intransitive or transitive verb that consists of two parts: a verb and a particle,


(e.g., _He looked up the word/He looked the word up. She gave up.)_


  - _**phrase**_


A string of words that behaves as a constituent and has a head as its principal element. •


  - _**phrase marker**_


A graphic representation of the structure of a (subpart of a) sentence, alternatively


known as a _tree_ or _tree diagram._


**282**  - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-282-full.png)

www.am oriteaching.co.kr^


° _**pitch**_


The perceptual correlate of the frequency of a sound. The higher the frequency of vocal


cord vibration, the higher the pitch.


- _**polysemy**_


One word-form with two related meanings (e.g., _bright_ means either ‘intelligent or


‘shining’).


- _**predicate**_


A semantic label which denotes an element that requires the specification of the


participants (arguments) in the proposition expressed, (e.g., in the sentence _We like_


_parties_ the predicate is the verb _like_ which requires a Subject argument and a Direct


Object argument).


- _**predicate)r**_


A functional label applied to the verb in a sentence or clause.


- _**prepositional verb**_


A verb that takes a prepositional phrase as its Complement, (e.g., _rely on NP, look_


_at NP)_


_**• presupposition**_


A proposition expressed in a sentence that is assumed to be true in order to judge


the truth of another sentence (e.g., _Hart passed the bar exam_ presupposes _Hart took_


_the bar exam)._


- _**presupposition trigger**_


A word or structure that presupposes the truth of a proposition or the speaker’s attitude


about it (e.g., _Why are you in a bad mood?_ presupposes _You are in a bad mood). •_


- _**pro-form substitution**_


A test for determining whether a structural unit is a head, bar, or phrasal category (e.g.,


_one typically_ substitutes for N-bar categories, as in _I studied more fo r the last French_


_quiz than for the first one)._


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **283**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-283-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**pseudocleft sentence**_


A sentence which conforms to the following pattern:


wh-item + ... + form of _be_ _**+**_ focus


(e.g., _What Janice did was laugh out loud.)_


 - _**raising**_


A process which involves the displacement of a noun phrase from the Subject position


of a subordinate nonfinite clause typically to the main clause Subject position. A raising


verb is a verb that can appear in a raising construction, (e.g., _Martova seems_ _**[_**_ _to_


_be happy])._


 - _**reduced vowel**_


A vowel that is pronounced with a centralized schwa ([o]) quality (e.g. the first vowel


of _photography_ and the second vowel of _photograph_ ).


 - _**referring expression**_


A linguistic expression which denotes a person or entiry in the real world, (e.g., in


a particular context of utterance the referring expression _Nick_ denotes an individual


with the name _Nick)._


 - _**reverses**_


The characteristic reverse relation is between terms describing movement, where one


term describes movement in one direction, — and the other the same movement in


the opposite direction,, (e.g., _inflate/deflate,_ _expand/contract, full/empty_ or


_knit/unravel_ **) .**


0 _**rhyme**_


The part of a syllable that follows the onset (e.g. /int/ in _print)._


 - _**right node raising**_


A displacement process exemplified by the following sentence:


Pete bought _, but Hans read _, today’s newspaper.


Here, the noun phrase _today’s newspaper,_ which acts as the Direct Object of both _buy_


and _read,_ has been moved to the right and is raised from a right node position.


**284** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-284-full.png)

[www.amoriteaching.co.kr](http://www.amoriteaching.co.kr)


0 _**root**_


The morpheme in a word that carries the major component of the word’s meaning and


belongs to a lexical category, (e.g., _**collect**_ in the word _**collections).**_


_**• segment**_


A unit of phonology corresponding roughly to “speech sound”; allophones, phonemes,


and morphophonemes are all segments. We interpret the physical speech signal in terms


of segments.


- _**selectional restrictions**_


Semantic constraints on the NPs that a particular lexical item can take (e.g., _**regret**_


requires a human subject).


- _**semantic feature**_


A dimension of sense typically having two values (e.g., [±human]).


- _**semantic broadening**_


The process in which the meaning of a word becomes more inclusive than its earlier


form, (e.g., _**barn**_ used to mean V/ _**place to store barley9).**_


_**• semantic narrowing**_


The process in which the meaning of a word becomes less inclusive than its earlier


form, (e.g., _**meat**_ used to mean _**‘any type of food’).**_


e _**sibilant**_


A fricative or an affricate that is produced with a high-frequency energy, usually by


means of a groove in the tongue (e.g. /s, z, /, 3, tf, (fe/). •


- _**sister**_


A relation between two nodes in a tree diagram. Node A and B are sisters if they


are directly dominated by the same node: e.g.,


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **285**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-285-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**sonorant**_

![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-285-0.png)


Any member of the class of nasals, liquids, and glides.


 - _**sonority sequencing principle**_


Principles underlying syllable structure: onsets must rise in sonority, codas fall in


sonority, and nuclei contain the sonority peak of the syllable.


 - _**source**_


A thematic role that denotes the location or entity from which something moves.


 - _**specialization**_


Semantic change in which the scope of a word narrows (e.g., _**deer**_ used to means _**"any**_


_**wild animal**_ ’).


 - _**specifier**_


In X-bar syntax, a modifier that always occurs first in a phrase, before an adjunct,


complement, or head (e.g., the determiner _**that**_ in the NP _**that tall math student).**_ The


term specifier designates a position (like subject, direct object, etc.), not a class of


words (like adjective, noun, etc.).


 - _**speech act**_


A locutionary act (i.e., the act of saying something) plus an illocutionary act (i.e., the


act of doing something). •


 - _**stative verb**_


A verb that expresses a state or condition rather than an action, (e.g., _**have, know, like,**_


_**need, own).**_


**286** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-286-full.png)
###### **lljjp**

w w w .am oriteaching.co.kr^sr


- _**stem**_


The base to which an affix is attached to create a more complex form that may be


another stem or a word, (e.g., _modification_ is the stem for _-s_ in the word _modifications)._


° _**stop**_


A consonant sound that is produced with a complete closure in some part of the vocal


tract, followed by an abrupt release (e.g. /p, t, k, b, d, g/).


- _**structural ambiguity**_


The condition whereby an expression can be assigned more than one syntactic structure


(e.g., _old men and women_ = old [men and women] or [old men] and women).


- _**subcategorization**_


The requirement of a predicate to take a category (or categories) of a particular type


as its Complement, (e.g., the verb _devour_ subcategories a noun phrase, because we


cannot leave out the NP in a sentence that contains this verb: _*The kids devoured.)_


The syntactic ‘needs’ of predicates are specified in subcategorisation frames.


- _**subcategorization frame**_


A graphic way of representing the subcategorisation requirements of a particular


predicate, (e.g., the subcategorisation frame for the verb _devour_ is as follows:)


_devour_ (verb)


[_, NP]


- _**subject dependent inversion**_


The elements inverted are the subject and another dependent of the verb. The latter is


usually a complement - most commonly a locative or an adjectival predicative


complement, (e.g., A bowl of fruit was on her desk —> On her desk was a bowl of fruit.) •


- _**substitution**_


The replacement of a word or string of words by a proform.


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **287**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-287-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


° _**suffix**_


Affix attached to the end of a word (e.g., _manly)._


_**• s up r as eg mentals**_


Phonetic features that apply to units greater than segments, such as stress, rhythm,


intonation.


 - _**syllabic**_


A major phonological feature that characterizes vowels.


 - _**syllabic consonant**_


A consonant that, in a particular case, functions as a syllabic nucleus (e.g. _Ini_ in _button_ **,**


/l/ in _bottle)._


_**• synonymy**_


Two words having the same sense (e.g., _student_ and _pupil)._


_**• synthetic sentence**_


One that can be judged true or false only by evaluating the state of affairs it describes


(e.g., _Al Gore was president of the U.S. Senate)._


_**• systematic gaps**_


Gaps in the occurring syllable structures of a language that result from the exclusion


of certain sequences, (e.g., _**pfordv**_ in English)


 - _**telic verb**_


A verb expressing something with a natural end point. An achievement or an


accomplishment verb is telic. •


 - _**tense**_


The term tense denotes a grammatical system which is used to locate situations in


time.


**288** - What English Language Teachers Should Know


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-288-full.png)

www.amoriteaching.co.kr”^


- _**thematic role**_


The relation of a noun phrase to the action described by a verb. Common thematic


roles include agent (doer of the action), patient (entity affected by the action),


beneficiary (entity for whose benefit the action occurs).


- _**theme**_


A thematic role which is carried by an entity that is moved by the action or event


denoted by the predicate in a proposition.


- _**tough-movement structure**_


A sentence that has an ease/difficulty adjective (e.g., _tough, fan, simple, easy, pleasant,_


_impossible, difficult_ ) followed by an infinitive whose understood subject is the subject


of the main clause, (e.g., _.John is easy to please_ ).


- _**tho ugh-mo vement**_


A syntactic process which can be used as a constituency test. It is exemplified by the


following sentence pair:


Though Ken usually is quite happy, today he is sad.


—*• [Quite happy] though Ken usually is _, today he is sad.


- _**tonic syllable**_


The syllable that carries the major pitch change.


- _**topicalization**_


The displacement of a phrase to a clause-initial position for emphasis or prominence,


(e.g., _Films,_ _**I**_ _enjoy_ _.)


- _**v-har deletion**_


The deletion of a V-bar constituent, as in the following example: _Henry will travel_


_to South America, but Anna wont_ _, where the V-bar _travel to South America_ has been


deleted. •


 - _**velar**_


An articulation involving the back of the tongue and the velum (e.g. /k, g, ij/).


Glossary of English Linguistics Terminology • **289**


![](윤도형2015_images/2015-윤도형-전공영어학.pdf-289-full.png)

What English Language Teachers Should Know


 - _**velarization**_


Raising the back of the tongue toward the velum, as in the dark /l/ (e.g. _**full).**_


_**• verb complementation**_


This term refers to the kinds of Complement(s) that a verb can take.


 - _**vowel reduction**_


A procee that reduces a full vowel, typically unstressed, to a schwa, (e.g., _photograph_


[fotograef] vs. _photography_ [fotagrofr]).


 - _**vowel epenthesis**_


Insertion of a vowel (typically [a]) to break up a series of consonants (e.g., _**pastty**_


[pestri] —* [pestari]).


 - _**weak/reduced form**_


The common, unstressed form of a function word (e.g. [dat] for _that)._


_**• wh-movement**_


The displacement of a phrase that contains a word that begins with the letters ‘wh’.


For example, in _Which film did you see?_ the wh-phrase _which film_ has been moved


from a position immediately following the verb to the beginning of the sentence.)


 - _**vp-preposing**_


The movement of a verb phrase to a sentence-initial or clause-initial position. For


example:


He said that he would go to Rio, and go to Rio he did _.


Here by VP _go to Rio_ has been fronted under VP-Preposing.


 - _**X-bar syntax**_


A theory of constituent structure that recognizes a structural unit intermediate in size


between a phrasal and a head category (e.g., N-bar or N' is an intermediate category


between NP and N).
